الم یعنی ایک تو مشائخ کی عبارات کا جوالحاصل اور خلاصہ تھا اس کو لے لیا و میلاً الى الضبط اور اس صورت میں چونکہ ضبط اقسام درست ہو جاتی ہے اس لئے میلان الی الضبط کیا اور جمیع کو اقسام نظم قرار دیا لیکن التفات ہونی چاہتے توان عبارات مختلفہ کا حاصل یہی ہے کہ یہ نظم بالنسبتہ الی المعنی کی تقسیم اور اقسام ہیں۔ بحمل قولهم آل یعنی مشائخ کے اس قول سے کہ یہ نظم اور معنی کے اقسام ہیں یہ مراد نہیں ہے کہ بعض بلکہ مراد و سی نظم دال علی المعنی ہے اسی طرح اقسام النظم والمعنی میں بھی نظم دال علی المعنی مراد ہے۔
And it was mentioned in its interpretation that matters were mentioned that indicate being a quality of meaning, restricted to established by nazm, meaning their establishment from nazm is intentional and primary, or they are not intended primarily but their establishment is secondary. This refers to ibarah al-nass and isharah al-nass. And established through the meaning of the text is dalaalah al-nass. And established beyond the text [requires] addition to the text, meaning for the establishment of some rulings one must add upon the text, and this addition is like a condition for the validity of the text; without it the meaning of the text cannot be correct. This is iqtidaa al-nass. Some scholars opined that the divisions of the fourth division are divisions of meaning, and the three divisions are divisions of nazm. And some opined that only dalaalah al-nass and iqtidaa al-nass are divisions of meaning, and the rest are divisions of nazm. The author (musannif) explicitly stated that all these divisions are divisions of nazm in relation to meaning. He took the essence and summary of the statements of the mashayikh, and inclined to regulation, as in this form the regulation of divisions becomes correct, so he inclined to regulation and made all of them divisions of nazm. But since in the divisions of the fourth division, terms like 'established by text' or 'stopping at expression' etc. are mentioned, these cannot be divisions of nazm because establishment or stopping at expression are attributes of meaning, not attributes of nazm. So the commentator (sharh) removed this question by saying: the division of the fourth division is that which indicates by way of expression and indication, and the indication of a quality is a word, so these divisions can be related to nazm. He did not pay attention to the different statements of the mashayikh but took the essence of meaning because difference of statements is the habit of the mashayikh, but the objective is one. It is necessary to abandon the difference of statements and pay attention to the one objective. The gist of these different statements is that this is a division of nazm in relation to meaning. Interpreting their statement that these are divisions of nazm and meaning, it is not meant that some divisions are nazm and some are meaning, but rather the intent of that statement is also that these are divisions of nazm indicating meaning. Just as they said that the Quran is nazm and meaning, in this statement nazm and meaning are not considered two separate things, but rather what is intended is nazm that indicates meaning. Similarly, in 'divisions of nazm and meaning' the intended meaning is nazm indicating meaning.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a dispute about the classification of the four categories of dalaalat (textual indications): ibarah al-nass (explicit text), isharah al-nass (textual allusion), dalaalah al-nass (textual implication), and iqtidaa al-nass (textual requirement). The issue is whether these are attributes of the nazm (text/order) or of the ma'na (meaning). Some scholars held that the fourth division (iqtidaa? Actually the text says 'the fourth division'—likely referring to the fourth type) is of meaning, while the first three are of nazm. Others held only two (dalaalah and iqtidaa) are of meaning. The author (musannif) clarifies that all are divisions of nazm in relation to meaning. The commentator (sharh) defends this by noting that even the expressions like 'established by text' are ultimately descriptions of nazm because they refer to how nazm conveys meaning. The key point is that when mashayikh say 'divisions of nazm and meaning', they do not mean separate entities but rather nazm that indicates meaning. This is analogous to saying the Quran is 'nazm and meaning' — what is intended is the nazm that indicates meaning, not two independent entities. The ruling belongs to the standard Hanafi usul position: the four dalaalat are divisions of nazm (text), not meaning.

Key Terms
نظم (nazm): The textual wording and order of the Qur'an, considered as the carrier of meaning.
معنی (ma'na): The meaning or concept conveyed by the nazm.
دلالة (dalaalah): The indication or implication of a text, one of the four types of dalaalat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) All are divisions of ma'na (meaning)
B) Some are divisions of nazm and some are divisions of ma'na
C) All are divisions of nazm in relation to meaning
D) They are not divisions of either nazm or ma'na

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies all four dalaalat as functions of nazm (text), not ma'na, ensuring rulings are tied to the text's wording.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
The Meaning of Nazm and Ma'na in Defining the Qur'an
Clarifying the mashayikh's statement that Qur'an is nazm and ma'na combined

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intended meaning of the phrase 'al-nazm wa al-ma'na jami'an' in the Hanafi definition of the Qur'an and apply the distinction between nazm as lafz (word) and nazm as arrangement to resolve a usul disagreement.

Core Idea
The Qur'an is nazm indicating meaning — not words and meaning as separate, but the verbal arrangement that conveys the meaning.

This passage delves into a key phrase used by Hanafi mashayikh in defining the Qur'an: 'al-nazm wa al-ma'na jami'an'. Often misunderstood as referring to two separate components, the author clarifies that it actually means 'the nazm that indicates meaning' (al-nazm al-dall 'ala al-ma'na). The passage defends the Hanafi position that i'jaz (inimitability) resides in the nazm, not in the meaning alone, and addresses a terminological issue: in the context of dividing nazm into 'amm, khass, and mushtarak, nazm is equated with lafz (single word). Through these discussions, you will see how precise usul analysis resolves apparent ambiguities in foundational definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its context
The passage centers on the mashayikh's statement 'al-nazm wa al-ma'na jami'an'. Recognize that this was a response to a specific misunderstanding arising from Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling on the permissibility of Persian recitation in prayer, which could imply that the Qur'an is only about meaning. The mashayikh's phrase affirms the integral role of nazm.
Be aware of the historical occasion: the statement was not an abstract definition but a refutation.
2
Trace the proof from the definition of the Qur'an
The author uses the universally accepted definition of the Qur'an (Arabic, written in the mushafs, transmitted by tawatur) to show that its defining attributes are attributes of the lafz (word) that conveys meaning, not of meaning alone. This supports that 'nazm' in the phrase refers to the utterance that carries meaning.
Focus on the logical link: these attributes are all of the lafz, so the essence of the Qur'an is its verbal form.
3
Analyze the i'jaz argument
I'jaz is presented as an extreme form of balaghah (eloquence), which is an attribute of nazm. The objection that i'jaz lies in meaning due to the profound depth of Qur'anic meanings (e.g., the many loads of commentary on al-Fatihah) is refuted by explaining that this very profundity is a direct consequence of the unique nazm; no other verbal arrangement can convey such meanings.
Observe how the Hanafi response uses the same evidence (vastness of meanings) to reinforce their position.
4
Examine the historical reason for the phrase
The mashayikh's phrase was a response to a misunderstanding that could arise from Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling. The author explicitly states that the mashayikh used 'al-nazm wa al-ma'na jami'an' to repel the doubt that the Qur'an is only meaning, while their actual intent was still 'nazm that indicates meaning'.
This shows that usul analysis is often contextual—understanding the historical occasion clarifies the intended meaning.
5
Evaluate the terminological issue: nazm as lafz
The author claims that in the context of dividing nazm into 'amm, khass, mushtarak, nazm means lafz (single word). The objection that nazm properly means 'arrangement' is answered by noting that these categories apply to individual words, not to the arrangement. A possible reinterpretation (ta'wil) is that the divisions are attributes of the words occurring in nazm, not of the nazm itself.
This step highlights the importance of contextual meaning: terms in usul can shift meaning based on the context of the discussion.

یہ کہ نظر حقیقتاً بمعنی شعر کے نہیں ہے بلکہ نظم کا حقیقی معنی جمع اللوفی المسلک یعنی موتیوں کو تاگے میں پرونا اسی مناسبت سے شعر پر بھی اطلاق ہوتا ہے علی سبیل المجاز اوالاستعمارہ اور لفظ کا خود حقیقی معنی
In [the phrase] 'al-nazm wa al-ma'na' as well, what is intended is 'nazm that indicates meaning'. This is decisive proof that in 'al-Qur'an and al-nazm wa al-ma'na', what is intended is nazm that indicates meaning. Summary of the proof: In the definition of the Noble Qur'an, what is stated—being Arabic, written in the mushafs, transmitted by tawatur—all these attributes are attributes of a word (lafz) that indicates meaning, not of the combination of word and meaning. Similarly, i'jaz (inimitability): the i'jaz of the Qur'an, which is a limit of its eloquence (balaghah), is related to balaghah, and balaghah is among the attributes that refer to the word, in the sense that it is beneficial for meaning. For when one intends that this is its clarification—that balaghah is an attribute that refers back to the nazm that indicates meaning—that is, when one intends to express a meaning with a certain composition, various purposes arise that require specific qualities and characteristics that must be considered in the nazm. For example, the denial of the addressee requires emphasis, and if confinement is intended, it requires qualities like precedence, etc. When these characteristics are observed in a manner appropriate to human capacity, the speech becomes eloquent. When it reaches a level where it is impossible to oppose it, that speech becomes miraculous. Thus, i'jaz in this case is an attribute of nazm, in the sense that it is beneficial for meaning; it is not an attribute of the combination of nazm and ma'na. In any case, regarding the Noble Qur'an, when it is said 'al-nazm wa al-ma'na jami'an', what is intended is 'al-nazm al-dall 'ala al-ma'na'. And it may be said: This is an objection that balaghah is an attribute of the word. Summary: The meaning and concept of the Qur'an itself is miraculous, because complete knowledge of the meaning of the Qur'an is beyond human capacity. As it is said: 'The commentary of al-Fatihah has (knowledge) of many loads' meaning its commentary is so abundant that it would be the load of many camels. So it is known that i'jaz is an attribute of meaning. The response: The summary of the response is that this too is the i'jaz of nazm, because the nazm of the Qur'an contains so many meanings that other expressions and words cannot bear those meanings. And the intent of the mashayikh: That is, the mashayikh regarding the Qur'an said 'huwa al-nazm wa al-ma'na jami'an'. What is meant by it is al-nazm al-dall 'ala al-ma'na; the combination of nazm and ma'na is not intended. But the mashayikh used this expression to repel the misconception that arose from the Imam's statement permitting Persian recitation in prayer—that the Qur'an is only meaning, nazm has no part. So the mashayikh, for repelling the misconception, said 'al-nazm wa al-ma'na jami'an', and the intent is that same nazm that indicates meaning. His statement: 'What is meant by nazm here is lafz.' It is not said: The author said that nazm means lafz, then the questioner objected: According to the interpretation of the verifiers, nazm is 'the arrangement of words ordered according to meanings'—that is, arranging words in accordance with the order of meanings, consistent in their indications—such that they correspond to what the situation requires. 'La tawalimani al-nutq'—that is, nazm is not the name for just uttering words continuously and finishing them with each other arbitrarily without regard to proportion and what the situation requires. That is not called nazm. Rather, it is 'al-alfaz al-murattabah' (ordered words) or nazm is ordered words in which the mentioned consideration is observed. Even if in 'qa qadha niaka min dhakaranee habib wa manzil' one says 'nafqa qifa min habib dhakaranee', that would be lafz but not nazm, because the order is disturbed. So according to these interpretations, it is not known that nazm is synonymous with lafz; rather, it appears that nazm means 'arrangement of words' or 'ordered words'. Then how can it be correct to say 'al-nazm huwa al-lafz'? We say: The summary of the response is that in this place of division, nazm is applied to the single word (al-lafz al-mufrad). Because it is divided into types: particular ('amm), general ('amm), and shared (mushtarak). These are divisions of the single word. Therefore, in this place, nazm cannot mean other than al-lafz al-mufrad. Unless it is said there is a reinterpretation (ta'wil) that these divisions could be of nazm in the sense of arrangement, etc. Summary: It may be said that what is meant by 'divisions of nazm' are the divisions that have a relation to nazm, in that they are attributes of the components of nazm and of the words that occur in the nazm, even if they are not attributes of the nazm itself. There is no harm, because 'amm and 'amm are conventionally applied to the described word (lafz mawsuf), not to nazm. Therefore, these divisions cannot be of the nazm itself, but they have a relation to nazm, so with the mentioned reinterpretation they can be called 'divisions of nazm'. Before [the questioner said]: The summary of the question is that just as the word [lafz] is applied to poetry, so its application to the expression of the Noble Qur'an would be disapproved of. Similarly, nazm is applied to poetry, and poetry is applied to the Noble Qur'an; this too would be disapproved. Therefore, it was not appropriate to choose al-nazm. So one should have avoided this as well. We say: The summary of the response is that nazm is not literally in the sense of poetry; rather, the real meaning of nazm is 'stringing beads on a thread'. By that analogy, it is also applied to poetry metaphorically or as a trope. And the word itself has a real meaning.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the phrase 'al-nazm wa al-ma'na' as used by the mashayikh in defining the Qur'an. The author begins by asserting that the correct interpretation is 'nazm that indicates meaning' (al-nazm al-dall 'ala al-ma'na), not the combination of nazm and ma'na as distinct entities. The proof is drawn from the standard definition of the Qur'an: it is Arabic, written in the mushafs, and transmitted by tawatur. All these attributes describe the lafz (the word or utterance) that conveys meaning, not the meaning itself. Similarly, i'jaz (inimitability) is shown to be a property of nazm because it is a form of balaghah (eloquence), which pertains to the arrangement of words (nazm) that conveys meaning effectively. The author illustrates this with examples: when expressing affirmation or negation, the arrangement must suit the purpose, and when it reaches a level of perfection unattainable by human effort, it becomes miraculous. Thus, i'jaz is attributed to nazm, not to the combination. An objection is raised: some argue that i'jaz lies in the meaning itself, given the profound depth of Qur'anic meanings, like the many loads of commentary on al-Fatihah. The response is that this very profundity is a result of the unique nazm of the Qur'an; no other verbal arrangement can convey such vast meanings. The author then explains the historical context: the mashayikh's statement 'al-nazm wa al-ma'na jami'an' was a response to a misunderstanding arising from Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling that Persian recitation in prayer is permissible, which might suggest that the Qur'an is only meaning without concern for the Arabic nazm. To counter this, the mashayikh affirmed that the Qur'an is both nazm and ma'na together, but their intended meaning is still 'nazm that indicates meaning'. The second part of the passage addresses a different issue: the author's claim that 'nazm' in this context means 'lafz' (word). A questioner objects that nazm properly means 'arrangement of words according to meaning', not just any word. The author responds that in this specific context—where nazm is divided into 'amm, khass, and mushtarak (general, specific, and shared terms)—the reference is to the single word (lafz mufrad), because these divisions are classifications of individual words. A possible reinterpretation (ta'wil) is mentioned: these divisions could be seen as attributes of the words occurring in nazm rather than of the nazm itself. Finally, a concern about the impropriety of using 'nazm' for the Qur'an because of its association with poetry is answered: nazm's literal meaning is 'stringing pearls', and its application to poetry is metaphorical. Thus, using it for the Qur'an is not inherently problematic.

Key Terms
نظم (nazm): In this passage, nazm refers either to the Arabic utterance (lafz) that indicates meaning, or in the second part, to a single word (lafz mufrad) in the context of division into 'amm, khass, mushtarak
لفظ (lafz): The articulated word or utterance, independent of its arrangement. Here it is equated with nazm in the context of discussing individual words.
اعجاز (i'jaz): The miraculous inimitability of the Qur'an, which is a property of its nazm (arrangement) that conveys meanings in a way beyond human capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the mashayikh use the phrase 'al-nazm wa al-ma'na jami'an' regarding the Qur'an?
A) To define the Qur'an literally as both words and meanings combined
B) To rebut the misconception that the Qur'an is only meaning, arising from Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling on Persian recitation
C) To emphasize that both nazm and ma'na are equally miraculous
D) To introduce a new classification of nazm in usul al-fiqh

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'al-nazm wa al-ma'na jami'an' means 'nazm that indicates meaning,' affirming that the Qur'an's essential nature is its Arabic wording conveying inimitability.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
Defining Nazm in the Context of Word Classification
Defining nazm as the individual word and responding to objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi usul defines 'nazm' as the individual word in the context of dividing words into specific, general, and common, and respond to the two objections raised against this definition.

Core Idea
Nazm means the individual word when dividing into specific, general, and common, not the arrangement.

In Usul al-Fiqh, precise terminology is crucial. The term 'nazm' (النظم) often means composition or arrangement, but in the specific context of classifying words as specific (khas), general ('amm), and common (mushtarak), the Hanafi commentators specify that nazm refers to the individual word (lafz mufrad). This lesson unpacks that definition and addresses two objections: one from the literary definition of nazm as ordered arrangement, and another from the possible disrespect of using the term nazm for the Qur'an. Understanding this distinction sharpens your ability to correctly identify terms in usul texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Claim
The commentator states that in this context, nazm means lafz (word). Note that this is a contextual definition, not the general meaning.
2
Understand the First Objection
An objector argues that nazm properly means ordered arrangement of words with consistent meanings, as per literary critics. This challenges the claim because if nazm is arrangement, it cannot be the individual word.
The objector cites definitions of nazm from rhetoric.
3
Follow the Response to the First Objection
The commentator responds that here nazm is applied to the individual word because the division (specific, general, common) applies to words, not arrangements. An alternative interpretation is that these categories are attributes of the words occurring in the nazm (composition), so they relate to nazm but are not its direct divisions.
This shows the Hanafi method of contextualizing terminology.
4
Understand the Second Objection
Using nazm for the Qur'an might suggest it is poetry (nazm often used for poetic verses), which is disrespectful.
5
Follow the Second Response
The commentator explains that nazm literally means stringing pearls, so using it for the Qur'an is respectful, implying the words are like pearls. This also shows the Hanafi attention to etiquette (adab).
The response uses literal etymology to defend the term.

It is upon the meaning. He said: The intended meaning of nazm here is the word. The objector objected that according to the interpretation of the investigators, nazm is the arrangement of words with ordered meanings, that is, arranging words in accordance with the order of meanings, consistent indications, matching the requirement of the situation. Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a definitional discussion from a Usul al-Fiqh commentary. The commentator clarifies that in this specific context of dividing words into specific, general, and common, 'nazm' refers to the individual word (lafz mufrad), not to the arrangement of words. He responds to two objections: first, that nazm properly means ordered arrangement, and second, that using the term nazm for the Qur'an might suggest poetry. The response to the first objection explains that here nazm is used for the individual word because it is being divided into categories applicable to words. An alternative interpretation is that these categories are attributes of the words occurring in the nazm (composition), not of the nazm itself. To the second objection, the commentator explains that nazm literally means stringing together pearls, and using it for the Qur'an is respectful, implying that the words of the Qur'an are like pearls. This analysis shows the Hanafi usul terminology where nazm can have different meanings based on context.

Key Terms
النظم (al-nazm): In this context, nazm means the individual word (lafz mufrad) as opposed to arrangement or composition.
اللفظ (al-lafz): Word; the basic unit of language.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Arrangement of words
B) The individual word
C) Poetry
D) The meaning

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the term 'nazm' is context-sensitive; in the division of words, it means the individual word.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
The Correct Order of the Four Divisions of Words
Exposition of the correct order of the four divisions and the critique of Fakhral-Islam's alternative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct order of the four divisions of words according to Sadr al-Shariah and evaluate the alternative order proposed by Fakhral-Islam, applying the principle that wad' precedes isti'mal.

Core Idea
The order of word divisions follows the logical priority: assignment (wad') first, then usage (isti'mal), then apparentness (zuhur), then mode of indication.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, scholars classify words in a specific sequence. The order is not arbitrary but reflects the logical steps from the original assignment of a word to a meaning, to its actual usage, to understanding whether the meaning is apparent or hidden, and finally to the mode of textual indication. This lesson unpacks the correct order as established by Sadr al-Shariah, contrasting it with Fakhral-Islam's alternative arrangement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four divisions and their bases
Recognize that the divisions are based on: wad' (assignment), isti'mal (usage), zuhur/khafa (apparentness/hiddenness), and mode of dalalah (indication).
These are the four aspects of words in usul.
2
Understand the author's order
The author (Sadr al-Shariah) presents the order: first division by wad', then by isti'mal, then by zuhur/khafa, then mode of dalalah.
3
Compare with Fakhral-Islam's order
Fakhral-Islam placed the division by zuhur/khafa before isti'mal, arguing that it relates to wad' and therefore should precede usage.
4
Analyze the reasoning on both sides
The author argues that zuhur/khafa after actual usage (bil-fi'l), while Fakhral-Islam considers it potentially (bil-quwwah) before usage. Each has a valid aspect.
The text says 'walikullin wajhuhu' – each has its own perspective.
5
Determine the preferred order
The author's order is considered more correct (asahh) because it follows the actual sequence in reality: wad' logically before isti'mal, and zuhur after isti'mal in external context.
This becomes the standard Hanafi position.

His saying: 'by virtue of its assignment to him' is an explanation of the four divisions, i.e., the author has presented a summary explanation of the four divisions. And in the word 'thumma' (then) in the second, third, etc., the application of 'thumma' indicates that the order according to the manner mentioned in the text is correct. Since Fakhral-Islam's divisions do not have this order, it is indicated that the order mentioned in the text is more correct than Fakhral-Islam's order. Because the preceding, etc. The reason for this mentioned order is that with regard to the word, the first and prior is the assignment (wad') of the word to a meaning, then secondly is the usage (isti'mal) in the meaning, then after that it becomes known whether the meaning used in the word is apparent (zahir) or hidden (khafi), then after that follows the discussion of the nature of the word's indication of the used meaning, i.e., whether by 'ibarat al-nass' or 'isharat al-nass'. So the order of divisions should follow the mentioned manner as the author did. And Fakhral-Islam advanced the division by apparentness of meaning, i.e., Fakhral-Islam postponed the division by usage and advanced the division by apparentness and hiddenness of meaning contrary to what the author did, considering that the disposal (tasarruf) in speech has two types: one is disposal in the word, which is making the word such that the meaning is established from it; that is the assignment of the word. And disposal in the meaning, i.e., specifying the meaning such that the word conveys it with apparentness or hiddenness. This is the intended making of the meaning as the object of assignment. Both disposals are related to assignment (wad'), and assignment is prior to usage because usage is dependent on assignment. Therefore, the division by usage should be postponed from both these divisions. Even as if it is considered that apparentness and hiddenness are first considered because they relate to assignment, and usage is considered after, so the division by usage should be postponed from the division by apparentness of meaning. The essential point is that the author considers apparentness and hiddenness of meaning in actuality and in the external context, and that is certainly after usage. Fakhral-Islam considered apparentness and hiddenness potentially and in the context of the mind and observation, and that is before usage. And each has its own aspect. So the word in relation to meaning, i.e., the word in relation to meaning, the first division by assignment has four types: specific (khas), general ('amm), shared (mushtarak), and interpreted (mu'awwal). This is the division among the folk (the scholars). The author did not consider mu'awwal in this division and dropped it from the degree of consideration. Because if it indicates, the reason for the division is that if a word indicates a single meaning, then either it is intended individually or by way of sharing among individuals: the first is khas, the second is 'amm. If it indicates multiple meanings, if one of those meanings gains a preference over others, it is mu'awwal; if no meaning gains preference and all are equal in intent, it is mushtarak. The author did not consider mu'awwal in this division; instead he counted the indefinite plural (jam' munakkar). The second division, which is by usage, according to which the four types are: literal (haqiqah), metaphorical (majaz), explicit (sarih), and implied (kinayah). Because if the word is used in its assigned meaning, it is haqiqah; if used in other than its assigned meaning, it is majaz. Both of these, if their intended meaning is apparent, are sarih; and if the intended meaning is concealed and hidden, it is called kinayah.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage discusses the correct order of the four divisions of words in usul al-fiqh. The author (Sadr al-Shariah) insists on the order: 1) division by wad' (assignment of words to meanings), 2) division by isti'mal (usage), 3) division by zuhur (apparentness) and khafa (hiddenness) of meaning, and 4) discussion of the mode of indication (dalalah) like 'ibarat al-nass and isharat al-nass. The commentator explains that Fakhral-Islam (al-Bazdawi) proposed a different order, placing the division by zuhur/khafa before the division by isti'mal. The author argues that wad' logically precedes isti'mal, and since zuhur/khafa relate to the meaning after usage (in actual external context), they should come after isti'mal. However, Fakhral-Islam considers zuhur/khafa potentially (bil-quwwah) in the mind before usage, hence his order. The passage then defines the first division: khas (specific) indicates one meaning individually; 'amm (general) indicates a meaning shared among individuals; mushtarak (equivocal) indicates multiple meanings equally; mu'awwal (interpreted) indicates multiple meanings with one preferred. The author, however, replaces mu'awwal with jam' munakkar (indefinite plural). The second division: haqiqah (literal), majaz (metaphorical), sarih (explicit meaning clear), and kinayah (implied meaning hidden). This is standard Hanafi terminology.

Key Terms
وضع (wad'): The original linguistic assignment of a word to a meaning.
استعمال (isti'mal): The actual usage of a word in speech, either in its assigned meaning or another.
ظہور (zuhur): The quality of the meaning being apparent and easily understood from the word.
خفا (khafa): The quality of the meaning being hidden and not immediately apparent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author (Sadr al-Shariah), what is the correct order of the four divisions of words?
A) Isti'mal, wad', zuhur/khafa, mode of dalalah
B) Wad', isti'mal, zuhur/khafa, mode of dalalah
C) Zuhur/khafa, wad', isti'mal, mode of dalalah
D) Mode of dalalah, zuhur/khafa, isti'mal, wad'

Key Takeaway
The correct order of divisions in Hanafi usul is wad', isti'mal, zuhur/khafa, mode of dalalah, as established by Sadr al-Shariah.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
beginner
7 min
Definitions of the First and Second Divisions of Words
Definitions of the first division (by wad') and second division (by isti'mal) with the author's substitution of mu'awwal with jam' munakkar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the four types of words in the first division (khas, 'amm, mushtarak, jam' munakkar) and the four types in the second division (haqiqah, majaz, sarih, kinayah), and distinguish between them using Hanafi usul criteria.

Core Idea
Words are first categorized by their assignment to meanings (khas, 'amm, mushtarak, jam' munakkar), then by their usage (haqiqah, majaz, sarih, kinayah).

After establishing the correct order of divisions, the text defines the actual categories within each division. The first division, based on assignment (wad'), includes khas (specific), 'amm (general), mushtarak (equivocal), and mu'awwal (interpreted) – though the author replaces mu'awwal with jam' munakkar (indefinite plural). The second division, based on usage (isti'mal), includes haqiqah (literal), majaz (metaphorical), sarih (explicit), and kinayah (implied). These definitions are foundational for analyzing any text in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two divisions
Recognize that the first division is by assignment (wad') and the second is by usage (isti'mal). The first involves how words are originally tied to meanings; the second involves how they are actually used in speech.
2
Define khas and 'amm
Khas indicates one specific meaning individually; 'amm indicates a meaning that applies to many individuals collectively (sharing among individuals).
Example: 'rajul' (a man) is khas; 'al-nas' (people) is 'amm.
3
Define mushtarak and mu'awwal/jam' munakkar
Mushtarak indicates multiple meanings equally without preference; if one meaning is preferred, it is mu'awwal. The author substitutes mu'awwal with jam' munakkar (indefinite plural) in the first division.
Jam' munakkar like 'rijalun' indicates an indefinite number of men, which is a type of 'amm in some views.
4
Define haqiqah and majaz
Haqiqah is a word used in its originally assigned meaning; majaz is used in a different meaning due to a connection (qarinah).
Example: 'asad' (lion) in haqiqah means the animal; in majaz it can mean a brave man.
5
Define sarih and kinayah
Sarih is a word whose intended meaning is apparent and clear; kinayah is a word whose intended meaning is hidden and needs inference.
Example: 'talaq' is sarih for divorce; 'anti khaliyah' (you are empty) is kinayah for divorce if context indicates.

His saying: 'by virtue of its assignment to him' is an explanation of the four divisions, i.e., the author has presented a summary explanation of the four divisions. And in the word 'thumma' (then) in the second, third, etc., the application of 'thumma' indicates that the order according to the manner mentioned in the text is correct. Since Fakhral-Islam's divisions do not have this order, it is indicated that the order mentioned in the text is more correct than Fakhral-Islam's order. Because the preceding, etc. The reason for this mentioned order is that with regard to the word, the first and prior is the assignment (wad') of the word to a meaning, then secondly is the usage (isti'mal) in the meaning, then after that it becomes known whether the meaning used in the word is apparent (zahir) or hidden (khafi), then after that follows the discussion of the nature of the word's indication of the used meaning, i.e., whether by 'ibarat al-nass' or 'isharat al-nass'. So the order of divisions should follow the mentioned manner as the author did. And Fakhral-Islam advanced the division by apparentness of meaning, i.e., Fakhral-Islam postponed the division by usage and advanced the division by apparentness and hiddenness of meaning contrary to what the author did, considering that the disposal (tasarruf) in speech has two types: one is disposal in the word, which is making the word such that the meaning is established from it; that is the assignment of the word. And disposal in the meaning, i.e., specifying the meaning such that the word conveys it with apparentness or hiddenness. This is the intended making of the meaning as the object of assignment. Both disposals are related to assignment (wad'), and assignment is prior to usage because usage is dependent on assignment. Therefore, the division by usage should be postponed from both these divisions. Even as if it is considered that apparentness and hiddenness are first considered because they relate to assignment, and usage is considered after, so the division by usage should be postponed from the division by apparentness of meaning. The essential point is that the author considers apparentness and hiddenness of meaning in actuality and in the external context, and that is certainly after usage. Fakhral-Islam considered apparentness and hiddenness potentially and in the context of the mind and observation, and that is before usage. And each has its own aspect. So the word in relation to meaning, i.e., the word in relation to meaning, the first division by assignment has four types: specific (khas), general ('amm), shared (mushtarak), and interpreted (mu'awwal). This is the division among the folk (the scholars). The author did not consider mu'awwal in this division and dropped it from the degree of consideration. Because if it indicates, the reason for the division is that if a word indicates a single meaning, then either it is intended individually or by way of sharing among individuals: the first is khas, the second is 'amm. If it indicates multiple meanings, if one of those meanings gains a preference over others, it is mu'awwal; if no meaning gains preference and all are equal in intent, it is mushtarak. The author did not consider mu'awwal in this division; instead he counted the indefinite plural (jam' munakkar). The second division, which is by usage, according to which the four types are: literal (haqiqah), metaphorical (majaz), explicit (sarih), and implied (kinayah). Because if the word is used in its assigned meaning, it is haqiqah; if used in other than its assigned meaning, it is majaz. Both of these, if their intended meaning is apparent, are sarih; and if the intended meaning is concealed and hidden, it is called kinayah.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage discusses the correct order of the four divisions of words in usul al-fiqh. The author (Sadr al-Shariah) insists on the order: 1) division by wad' (assignment of words to meanings), 2) division by isti'mal (usage), 3) division by zuhur (apparentness) and khafa (hiddenness) of meaning, and 4) discussion of the mode of indication (dalalah) like 'ibarat al-nass and isharat al-nass. The commentator explains that Fakhral-Islam (al-Bazdawi) proposed a different order, placing the division by zuhur/khafa before the division by isti'mal. The author argues that wad' logically precedes isti'mal, and since zuhur/khafa relate to the meaning after usage (in actual external context), they should come after isti'mal. However, Fakhral-Islam considers zuhur/khafa potentially (bil-quwwah) in the mind before usage, hence his order. The passage then defines the first division: khas (specific) indicates one meaning individually; 'amm (general) indicates a meaning shared among individuals; mushtarak (equivocal) indicates multiple meanings equally; mu'awwal (interpreted) indicates multiple meanings with one preferred. The author, however, replaces mu'awwal with jam' munakkar (indefinite plural). The second division: haqiqah (literal), majaz (metaphorical), sarih (explicit meaning clear), and kinayah (implied meaning hidden). This is standard Hanafi terminology.

Key Terms
خاص (khas): A word that indicates one specific meaning individually.
عام ('amm): A word that indicates a meaning shared among all individuals of its category.
مشترک (mushtarak): A word that indicates multiple meanings equally without preference.
ماول (mu'awwal): A word used in a non-literal meaning due to preponderance of one meaning over others.
جمع منکر (jam' munakkar): An indefinite plural noun, e.g., 'rijalun' (men).
حقیقت (haqiqah): A word used in its original assigned meaning.
مجاز (majaz): A word used in a meaning other than its original assignment due to a connection.
صریح (sarih): A word whose intended meaning is apparent and unambiguous.
کنایہ (kinayah): A word whose intended meaning is hidden and requires inference.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second division (by usage), a word used in its original assigned meaning is called: A) Majaz B) Haqiqah C) Sarih D) Kinayah
A) Majaz
B) Haqiqah
C) Sarih
D) Kinayah

Key Takeaway
The first division (by wad') yields khas, 'amm, mushtarak, jam' munakkar; the second division (by isti'mal) yields haqiqah, majaz, sarih, kinayah.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
15 min
The Fourfold Division of Lafz in Hanafi Usul
Expansion of four usul divisions with objection and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four divisions of lafz (by wad', isti'mal, wuduh/khafa', and dalalat), classify a given word under each division, and explain why these divisions are not mutually exclusive.

Core Idea
A single word can be classified multiple ways depending on which aspect of its relationship to meaning is considered.

This passage from a Hanafi usul commentary presents the four classical divisions of lafz, each based on a different criterion: wad' (coining), isti'mal (usage), wuduh/khafa (clarity/obscurity), and dalalat (mode of indication). Understanding these divisions is essential for deriving rulings from texts, as each division informs how a word is interpreted in legal contexts.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two preliminary dispositions (tasarruf)
The passage first distinguishes between two types of disposition: tasarruf fi al-lafz (making the word a wad' for a meaning) and tasarruf fi al-ma'na (making the meaning a subject of the word, either by zuhur or khafa'). Note that wad' precedes isti'mal because isti'mal depends on wad'. This sets the stage for the order of divisions.
The author notes that some scholars consider zuhur/khafa as part of wad', not isti'mal.
2
Master the first division: by wad'
Identify the four types based on wad': khas (specific), 'amm (general), mushtarak (homonym), and mu'awwal (interpreted). However, the author omits mu'awwal and substitutes jamm munakkar (indefinite plural). The criterion: does the word indicate one meaning or multiple? If multiple, is one preferred (mu'awwal) or equal (mushtarak)?
The omission of mu'awwal is a Hanafi-specific choice; other schools may include it.
3
Master the second division: by isti'mal
Learn four types based on usage: haqiqah (literal), majaz (metaphorical), sarih (explicit), kinayah (implicit). The criterion: is the word used in its original meaning? And is the intended meaning apparent? Haqiqah is used in its original meaning; majaz is used in a non-original meaning. Sarih has a clear intended meaning; kinayah has a hidden intended meaning.
A word can be haqiqah and kinayah simultaneously.
4
Master the third division: by wuduh/khafa'
This is the clarity hierarchy. On the apparent side: zahir (clear, admits ta'wil), nass (explicit, no ta'wil), mufassar (explicit and abrogable), muhkam (explicit and non-abrogable). On the obscure side: khafi (hidden due to wording), mushkil (can be grasped with deep thought), mujmal (needs clarification), mutashabih (no hope of clarity). Understand the branching logic: if meaning is apparent, check for ta'wil and abrogation; if hidden, check if it can be attained.
The Hanafi hierarchy differs from the Shafi'i in the placement of some categories.
5
Master the fourth division: by dalalat
Identify the four modes of indication: 'ibar al-nass (directly by wording), isharat al-nass (indicated but not main purpose), dalalat al-nass (by linguistic implication), iqtida' al-nass (by ellipsis). The key is whether the word directly and intentionally signifies the meaning (ibar), or the meaning is implied (ishara, dalala) or necessary for validity (iqtida').
These four are collectively known as the 'four dala'il' of nass.
6
Address the objection: why aren't these divisions mutually exclusive?
A student objects that a word like 'ayn can be both mushtarak (first division) and 'amm (first division) — but that is within the same division. The objector also means across divisions: 'ayn is mushtarak by wad' but also could be considered in other divisions. The author replies that these are separate divisions based on different i'tibarat (considerations). Exclusivity is required within each division, not across divisions.
This clarifies that classification is perspective-dependent.

يقيم منه المعنى یعنی لفظ کو اس طرح متعین کرنا کہ اس سے معنی سمجھ میں آجائے یہ وہی وضع اللفظ ہے۔ یعنی جعل اللفظ موضوعا د و سراقم ہے تصرف فی المعنی یعنی معنی کو بایں طور معین اور مخصوص کرنا کہ لفظ سے وہ سمجھ آجائے بالظهور والحفار یعنی اسی تصرف معنوی میں یہ معتبر ہوتا ہے کہ یہ معنی اپنے لفظ سے علی سبیل الظہور سمجھ آئیگا یا علی سبیل الحفار یہی مراد ہے معنی کو موضوع لہ بنانا یعنی جعل المعنی موضوعا اور یہ دونوں تصرف متعلق بالوضع ہیں اور وضع مقدم على الاستعمال ہے کیونکہ استعمال مترتب على الوضع ہے لہذا تقسیم بحسب الاستعمال کو ان دونوں سے موخر ہونا چاہتے حتی کانه لوحظ الخ یعنی گویا کہ ظہور معنی اور خفا اولاً ملحوظ ہوتا ہے کیونکہ اس کا تعلق وضع کے ساتھ ہے اور استعمال اس کے بعد ملحوظ ہوتی ہے اس لئے تقسیم بحسب الاستعمال کو تقسیم باعتبار ظہور المعنی سے مؤخر ہونا چاہئے اصل بات یہ ہے کہ مصنف نے ظہور اور خفاء معنی بالفعل اور فی ظرف الخارج اعتبار کیا ہے اور یہ یقینا بعد الاستعمال ہے اور فخر الاسلام نے ظہور معنی اور خفا بالقوۃ اور فی ظرف الذین والملاحظہ کا اعتبار کیا ہے اور یہ قبل الاستعمال ہے ولکل وجھہ ھو مولیہا ۔ فا اللفظ بالنسبة الى المعنى الا يعنى لفظ بالنسبة الى المعنی تقسیم اول کے لحاظ سے یعنی تقسیم باعتبار وضع کے چار قسم ہے خاص - عام - مشترک ماول یہ تقسیم عند القوم ہے مصنف نے اس تقسیم میں ماول کو اعتبار نہیں کیا اور اس کو ساقط عن درجة الاعتبار کر دیا۔ لانه ان دل یہ وجہ تقسیم ہے خلاصہ اس کا یہ ہے کہ لفظ اگر معنی واحد پر دال ہو تو پھر یا تو یعنی على الانفراد مراد ہوگا یا علی سبیل الاشتراک بین الافراد پہلا قسم خاص ہے دوسرا عام دان دل علی معان اگر اس لفظ کی دلالت معانی متعددہ پر ہو اگر ان میں سے کوئی معنی ترجیح علی البعض حاصل کرلے تو ماؤل اگر کوئی معنی ترجیح حاصل نہ کرے بلکہ تمام متساوی المراد ہوں تو مشترک ۔ مصنف نے اس تقسیم میں ماڈل کو اعتبار نہیں کیا اس کے عوض جمع منکر کو شمار کر لیا ہے۔ التقسيم الثانی دوسری تقسیم جو باعتبار استعمال کے تھی اس کے لحاظ سے اقسام اربعہ یہ ہوں گے حقیقہ مجاز صریح ۔ کنایہ لانه ان استعمل الخمر یہ وجہ تن رتقي نسیم ہے کہ لفظ اگر اپنے موضوع الہ معنی میں مستعمل ہو تو حقیقہ اگر غیر موضوع لہ میں مستعمل ہو تو وہ مجاز ہے یہ ہر دو نوں اگر ہر ایک کی مراد ظاہر ہو تو صریح ہے اور اگر اس کی مراد مستتر اور مخفی ہو تو اس کو کنایہ کہتے ہیں۔ وبالتقسيم الثالث اور تیسری تقسیم کے لحاظ سے منقسم ہو گا ظاہر نص مفسر محکم اور ان کے مقابلات خفی - مشکل وغیرہ کی طرف لانه ان ظهر معناه الخ یہ وجہ تقسیم و حصر ہے کہ اگر لفظ کا معنی ظاہر ہے تو پھر اس کے اندر احتمال تاویل ہو گا یا نہیں خان احتمل التاویل اگر اس کے اندرا حتمال تاویل ہے پھر اگر اس کا ظہور معنی صرف اور محض صیغہ سے ہے تو اس کو ظاہر کہتے ہیں ورنہ تو نص وان لم يحتمل اگر احتمال تاویل نہ ہو تو پھر اگر قابل نسخ ہو تو وہ مفسر ہے اگر قابل نسخ نہ ہو تو وہ محکم ہے دان خفی معناہ یہ تقسیم الی المقابلات ہے یعنی اگر اس لفظ معنی ظاہر نہیں ہے بلکہ خفی ہے تو فاما ان یکون یعنی اس کا خفا صیغہ کی وجہ سے ہوگا پھر خان امکن اور الہ یعنی غور فکر کے ساتھ یا تو اس معنی کا ادراک ممکن ہو گا تو اسے مشکل کہتے ہیں والا یعنی غور فکر کے ساتھ اگر ادراک معنی ممکن نہیں ہے تو فان كان البیان پھر اگر تبیین اور توضیح معنی کی امید در جا ہے تو تو اس کو مجمل کہتے ہیں اگر امید بیان نہیں ہے تو اس کو متشابہ کہتے ہیں وبالتقسيم الرابع یعنی تقسیم رابع کے لحاظ سے لفظ منقسم ہو گا اربعہ اقسام کی طرف ایک دال بطريق العبارة دوسرا دال بطريق الاشارہ تیسرا قسم وال بطریق الدلالہ چوتھا دال بطریق الاقتضا یہ وہی مشہور اقسام ہیں عبارة النص - اشارة النص - دلالۃ النص اور اقتضاء النص لانه ان دل على المعنى بالنظم یہ وجہ تقسیم و حصر ہے خلاصہ یہ کہ لفظ اگر دال علی المعنی بالنظم ہے پھر اگر وہ اسی معنی مدلول کی خاطر مسوق ہے اور سیاق کلام اسی معنی کے لئے ہے تو اسے عبارة النص کہتے ہیں اگر اس کی خاطر مسوق نہیں ہے تو اسے اشارۃ النص کہتے ہیں ۔ فان دل عليه بالمفهوم یعنی اگر اس لفظ کی دلالت بالنظم واللفظ نہیں ہے بلکہ اس کی دلالت باعتبار مفہوم لغوی کے ہے تو وہ دلالة النص والأفالاقتضاء الى اقتضاء النص والعمدة اصل شئی تو ان اقسام کے متعلق استقراء ہے جو مذکور ہے ۔ فان قلت خلاصہ سوال یہ کہ تقسیم سے جو اقسام پیدا ہوتے ہیں ان میں ضروری ہوتا ہے کہ ایک دوسرے کے ساتھ مختلف اور متباین ہوں جو آپس میں جمع نہ ہو سکیں ۔ نصر منتف اور یہ چیز ان اقسام میں منتفی ہے کیونکہ یہ اقسام ایک دوسرے پر صادق آتے ہیں جیسے لفظ عین مشترک تبھی ہے اور عام بھی قلت خلاصہ جواب یہ کہ یہ کوئی ایک تقسیم نہیں ہے بلکہ بحسب الاعتبارات المختلفہ یہ تقسیمات متعددہ ہیں یعنی سر ایک تقسیم دوسری سے الگ ہے لیندا تقسیمات کے جمیع اقسام میں تبائین اور اختلاف کا ہونا ضروری نہیں البتہ ہر ایک ایک تـ
Establishing the meaning from it means: making the word determined such that the meaning is understood from it. This is precisely wad' al-lafz, i.e., making the word a subject [of meaning]. And 'sarqam' is a disposition in the meaning, i.e., specifying and particularizing the meaning such that it is understood from the word by way of clarity (zuhur) or obscurity (khafa'). This intended meaning is making the meaning a subject of the word, i.e., making the meaning subject. Both dispositions relate to wad'. Wad' is prior to isti'mal because isti'mal depends on wad'. Therefore the division by isti'mal should be postponed after both of these, as if it were observed first... [continues similarly for each division]
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the Hanafi classification of lafz (word) in relation to its meaning. The author first distinguishes between two types of tasarruf (disposition): tasarruf fi al-lafz (making the word a wad' for a meaning) and tasarruf fi al-ma'na (making the meaning a subject of the word, either by zuhur or khafa'). He notes that wad' precedes isti'mal, so divisions based on isti'mal should come after those based on wad' and zuhur/khafa. He then presents four divisions:

1. **Division by wad' (first division):** Four types: khas (specific), 'amm (general), mushtarak (homonym), mu'awwal (interpreted). The author omits mu'awwal and substitutes jamm munakkar (indefinite plural?) - this may be a Hanafi specific addition.
2. **Division by isti'mal (second division):** Four types: haqiqah (literal), majaz (metaphorical), sarih (explicit), kinayah (implicit). If the word is used in its original meaning, it's haqiqah; if other than its original, it's majaz. If either's intended meaning is apparent, it's sarih; if hidden, it's kinayah.
3. **Division by wuduh/khafa (third division):** The apparent side: zahir (clear, but admits ta'wil), nass (explicit, no ta'wil), mufassar (explicit and abrogable), muhkam (explicit and non-abrogable). Their opposites: khafi (hidden due to wording), mushkil (difficult but attainable with thought), mujmal (needs bayan), mutashabih (no hope of clarity). This is the standard Hanafi clarity hierarchy.
4. **Division by dalalat (fourth division):** Four ways a word indicates meaning: 'ibar al-nass (directly by wording), isharat al-nass (indicated but not main purpose), dalalat al-nass (by linguistic implication), iqtida' al-nass (by ellipsis/necessity).

Finally, a student objects that these divisions are not mutually exclusive (e.g., 'ayn is both mushtarak and 'amm). The author replies that these are separate divisions based on different considerations (i'tibarat), so exclusivity within each division is sufficient.

Key Terms
وضع (wad'): Assigning a word to a meaning by convention or original coinage.
ظاہر (zahir): A word whose meaning is clear but admits ta'wil (interpretive possibility).
نص (nass): A word whose meaning is explicit and does not admit ta'wil.
عبارة النص (ibarat al-nass): The direct meaning derived from the wording explicitly.
خاص (khas): A word indicating a specific individual or limited category.
عام ('amm): A word indicating all individuals of a category without restriction.
مشترک (mushtarak): A word indicating multiple meanings with equal likelihood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third division (by wuduh/khafa'), if a word's meaning is hidden due to the wording itself but can be grasped with deep thought, it is called:
A) Khafi
B) Mushkil
C) Mujmal
D) Mutashabih

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fourfold division of lafz—by wad', isti'mal, wuduh/khafa', and dalalat—provides a systematic framework for analyzing words from different angles, each with its own categories and criteria.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
6 min
Distinguishing Mushtarak and 'Amm in Hanafi Usul
Commentary on Fakhru al-Islam's division of lafz by wad'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mushtarak and 'amm by applying the Hanafi criterion of wad' to a given lafz.

Core Idea
Mushtarak has multiple wad's; 'Amm has one wad' covering all suitable individuals.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the classification of words (lafz) according to their linguistic convention (wad') is foundational for deriving rulings. This lesson sharpens your ability to differentiate two key categories: mushtarak (homonym) and 'amm (general). The passage from al-Tawdih clarifies that mushtarak arises from multiple separate wad's for different meanings, while 'amm arises from a single wad' that encompasses all individuals to which it can apply. Mastering this distinction prevents misapplication of texts in istinbat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division being discussed
The passage deals with the first division of lafz according to wad'. Recognize that wad' includes both the matter (maddah) and form (hay'ah) of the word.
Fakhru al-Islam's phrase 'sigha wa lugha' is interpreted as referring to wad'.
2
Define mushtarak
A word is mushtarak if it has been placed for multiple meanings with multiple separate wad's. Each meaning has its own independent convention. Example: 'ayn (eye, gold, balance-tongue).
Mushtarak requires context (qarinah) to determine which meaning is intended.
3
Define 'amm
A word is 'amm if it has been placed for many unlimited meanings with a single wad', and it must be comprehensive (mustaghriq) for all individuals upon which it is valid to apply. Example: 'water' covers all types of water with one wad'.
If it does not encompass all, it becomes jami' munkar, not 'amm.
4
Apply the distinction to a concrete word
Take the word 'ayn from the passage. From one aspect (meaning the seeing organ), it is 'amm because it includes all individuals that see. From another aspect (meanings for eye, gold, knee), it is mushtarak due to multiple wad's.
The same word can be both depending on the aspect considered.
5
Recognize the condition of comprehensiveness for 'amm
For a word to be 'amm, it must be comprehensive: it must include every individual that is suitable for the meaning. The passage stresses 'bi-shart an yastaghriqa jami' ma yasluhu lahu'.
This condition is what separates 'amm from indefinite plurals (jami' munkar).

البیان مراد اس سے طرق استعمال ہیں کہ لفظ اگر اپنے موضوع لہ میں استعمال ہے تو حقیقہ اگر غیر موضوع لہ میں ہے تو مجاز ہوگا اور وجوہ الجریان سے لیہ تو اگر ہے تو مجاز اور سے مراد یہ کہ نظم معنی کے بیان اور اظہار میں کس طریق پر جاری ہے اگر تبیین معنی بطریق الوضوح ہے تو یہ صریح ہو گا اگر بطریق الاستتار ہے تو کنایہ ہو گا ۔ وعن الثالث اور تیسری تقسیم کو تعبیر کیا اپنے قول فی وجوہ البیان بذلک النظم جس سے مراد ہے معانی کے طرق اظہار اور اس ظہور کے مراتب اور چوتھی تقسیم کے متعلق کہانی معرفہ وجوہ الوقوف على المراد والمعانی یعنی سامع کا مراد متکلم اور معافی کلام پر واقفیت اور اطلاع حاصل کرنے کے طریقے کہ اطلاع بطریق عبارة ہے یا اشارہ وغیرہ ۔ قال المصنف في التوضح التقسيم الأول الى الذي باعتبار وضع اللفظ للمعنى یعنی یہ پہلی تقسیم ہے جو باعتبار وضع لفظ کے تھی تو تقسیم کو پیش کرتے ہوئے کہا اللفظ ان وضع للكثير یعنی اگر لفظ کی وضع کثیر معانی کے لئے بوضع متعدد ہے بایں طور کہ ہر معنی کے لئے الگ الگ وضع ہے ۔ نشترك تو اسے مشترک کہتے ہیں کا تعین جیسے لفظ عین کہ اس کی ایک وضع باصرہ آنکھ کے لئے ہے اور دوسری ذهب سونے کے لئے تیسری عین المیزان یعنی ترازو کی وہ جز جس کے ساتھ اس کی استقامت اور وزن کی صحت معلوم ہوتی ہے۔ او وضعا واحدا یعنی اس لفظ کی وضع معانی کثیرہ کے لئے باوضاع کثیرہ متعددہ نہیں بلکہ بوضع واحد معانی کثیرہ کے لئے موضوع ہے والکثیر غیر محصور اگر یہ معانی کثیرہ جن کے لئے لفظ کی وضع واحد ہے۔ غیر محصور ہوں یعنی کسی حصر واحاطہ کے تحت محصور نہ ہوں نعام تو عام ہے بشرطیکہ جمیع ما يصلح لہ کو مستغرق ہو یعنی جن افراد پر صادق آنیکی صلاحیت رکھتا ہے ان جمیع کو مستغرق اور شامل ہو والا اگر جمیع یا یصلح لہ کے لئے مستغرق نہیں ہے ۔ نجمع منگر تو وہ جمع منکر ہے۔ فالعام لفظ تو عام ایسے لفظ کا نام ہے جس کی وضع بوضع واحد
From which the categories that emerge must be mutually exclusive, and this is present in every one of us. This is what you see: this is a presentation that clarifies the preceding. The conclusion is that according to one division, the noun is divided into mu'rab and mabni, and according to another division it is divided into ma'rifa and nakira, even though both ma'rifa and nakira are either mu'rab or mabni. However, the categories that emerge from one division cannot be combined with each other. But if one makes [the opposite], this is a second answer. In summary, if the objective is to compare all the categories of all divisions, then the differences in aspects and considerations will suffice to differentiate them, i.e., by considering differences in aspects, then all categories will become mutually exclusive. For example, from the categories of the first division, consider the word 'ayn: from one aspect that it means the seeing organ, including all individuals that see, it is 'amm; if from another aspect you see that its wad' is for the eye, and also for gold, knee, etc., then from this aspect it is mushtarak, not 'amm. Similarly, these categories become mutually exclusive and different from each other with aspects. Likewise the second division, etc. His statement: 'And this is what Fakhru al-Islam said concerning the first division.' That is, regarding the first division, Fakhru al-Islam's expression is: 'The first is in terms of the patterns: sigha and lugha.' So some said that sigha and lugha are synonymous and have the same meaning, as it is said that by lugha the compositional matter of the word is meant, and by sigha its form resulting from movements and pauses, and due to these, the word indicates the meaning. Therefore, the purpose is the division of the pattern according to the meaning itself, in which the speaker and listener have no role, as in some divisions of clarity of meaning or concealment of meaning according to the speaker and listener. The closest is what the author mentioned: the interpretation of Fakhru al-Islam's expression with his own words 'bi i'tibar wad'ihi lahu' is closer. So the purpose is the division of the word according to wad'. Because sigha is evidence of that intent. In summary, sigha is the name of the form that occurs in the word due to movements and pauses and due to the order of letters, i.e., priority of some over others. And lugha is the word placed, but here by lugha is meant the matter of the word and the essence of letters, i.e., the letters from which the composition and arrangement of the word are made. By the context of its combination, i.e., combining the word with sigha, it is indicated that by lugha is meant the compositional matter. Since sigha meant the accidental form, its counterpart lugha means the compositional matter. The wad' of 'daraba' placed the compositional letters for the specific meaning of hitting, and similarly its form, obtained from the three movements, was specifically placed for the meaning of past tense. So the word cannot indicate its meaning without the placing of matter and form. Therefore, by mentioning sigha and lugha, the intent is wad' al-lafz. And he expressed the second division: Fakhru al-Islam expressed the second division with these words: 'The second division is in terms of the modes of using that pattern and its course in the chapter of clarification.' By this is meant the modes of usage: if the word is used in its original meaning, it is haqiqa; if in a non-original meaning, it is majaz. And by 'its course' is meant how the pattern proceeds in expressing and clarifying the meaning; if clarification is by way of clarity, it is sarih; if by way of concealment, it is kinaya. And concerning the third: a third division expressed by his statement 'in terms of the modes of clarification by that pattern', by which is meant the modes of expressing meanings and the levels of that clarity. And regarding the fourth division, he said 'in terms of the modes of understanding the intended meanings' i.e., the way the listener gains acquaintance and information about the speaker's intent and the meaning of the speech: whether information is by way of 'ibara or ishara etc. The author said in al-Tawdih: 'The first division is that which is according to the placing of the word for the meaning.' That is, this is the first division that was according to the wad' of the word. So presenting the division, he said: 'Al-lafz: if it is placed for many meanings with multiple wad's, in such a way that for each meaning there is a separate wad', it is mushtarak.' For example, the word 'ayn: one wad' for the seeing eye, a second for gold, a third for the tongue of the balance. 'Or with one wad'' i.e., the word's placement for many meanings is not with multiple wad's but with a single wad' for many meanings. 'And the many are unlimited': if these many meanings for which the word is placed with one wad' are unlimited, i.e., not confined under any limitation, then it is 'amm, provided it encompasses all that is suitable for it, i.e., it includes and covers all individuals upon which it is valid to apply. And if it does not encompass all that is suitable for it, then it is a definite plural. So al-'amm is a word whose wad' is with a single wad'.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on al-Tawdih, explaining the first division of lafz (word) according to wad' (linguistic convention). The author first clarifies a preliminary issue: categories from different divisions must be mutually exclusive, but they can be considered from different aspects to avoid conflict. Then he presents Fakhru al-Islam's expression of the first division in terms of 'sigha' and 'lugha', and explains that these two terms ultimately refer to wad'. The commenter argues that the author's interpretation (bi i'tibar wad'ihi lahu) is more accurate. He then defines sigha as the form (hay'ah) and lugha as the matter (maddah) of the word. He illustrates with the verb 'daraba', whose maddah is placed for hitting and whose hay'ah (with three vowels) indicates past tense. Thus, wad' encompasses both matter and form. The passage then lists the four divisions as per Fakhru al-Islam: first division by wad', second by usage (haqiqa/majaz), third by clarity (sarih/kinayah), and fourth by understanding the intended meaning ('ibarah/isharah etc.). Finally, it zooms into the first division: if a word is placed for many meanings with multiple separate wad's, it is mushtarak (e.g., 'ayn for eye, gold, balance-tongue). If placed for many unlimited meanings with a single wad', it is 'amm, provided it encompasses all suitable individuals. If it does not encompass all (like a definite plural with limitation), it is jami' munkar (indefinite plural). Thus, the key distinction is between mushtarak (multiple wad') and 'amm (single wad' covering many).

Key Terms
المشترک (al-mushtarak): A word placed for multiple meanings with multiple separate linguistic conventions (wad's).
العام (al-'amm): A word placed for many unlimited meanings with a single wad', provided it encompasses all suitable individuals.
الوضع (al-wad'): The linguistic convention placing a word for a meaning; here includes both matter (maddah) and form (hay'ah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes mushtarak from 'amm in the first division of lafz by wad'?
A) Mushtarak has one wad' for many meanings, 'amm has multiple wad's.
B) Mushtarak has multiple wad', 'amm has one wad' covering unlimited meanings with comprehensiveness.
C) Both have multiple wad', but 'amm also requires comprehensiveness.
D) Mushtarak is only for physical objects, 'amm is for abstract concepts.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, mushtarak has multiple wad's; 'amm has one wad' covering all fit individuals.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Definition of 'Aam (General Word) and Its Exclusions
Defining 'aam and excluding mushtarak, mahsur, and jama' munkar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'aam, identify its four restrictive conditions, and distinguish it from mushtarak, jama' munkar, and mahsur.

Core Idea
'Aam is a word coined for unlimited instances by one coining — the conditions separate it from homonyms, limited plurals, and indefinite plurals.

The Hanafi school builds its understanding of Quranic and hadith texts on precise linguistic categories. One of the most foundational is al-'aam (the general word). In this passage, the author of al-Tawdih defines 'aam by breaking down its essential conditions and excluding similar categories. Master these exclusions and you will never confuse 'aam with mushtarak, mahsur, or jama' munkar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The term is 'aam (general). It is a category of lafz (word) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.
The definition is purely linguistic; no scriptural proof is given here.
2
State the four conditions
The four muqayyadat (restrictions) are: (1) wad'an wahidan (single coining), (2) al-kathir (for many), (3) ghayri mahsur (unlimited), (4) mustaghriq li-jami'i ma yaslahu lahu (encompassing all valid individuals).
Each condition excludes a specific type of word.
3
Work through each exclusion
Condition (1) excludes mushtarak (homonyms like 'ayn). Condition (2) excludes words not coined for many, like proper nouns (Zayd). Condition (3) excludes limited plurals like numbers (mi'a). Condition (4) excludes indefinite plurals like rijalan.
Indefinite plurals do not encompass all individuals and thus fail condition (4).
4
Note the dispute on jama' munkar
Some scholars hold that indefinite plurals can be 'aam unless a qarina restricts; others (including the author) hold they are never 'aam but intermediate between 'aam and khas.
This disagreement affects how one interprets certain Quranic and hadith texts.
5
Distinguish from khas
Any word that fails any condition is khas (specific), including limited plurals, proper nouns, and singular common nouns. Even singular common nouns like 'rajul' are khas because they are not coined for many.
Khas includes both words for a single person and words for a single type/class.

Source Text

رکھتا ہے ان جمیع کو مستغرق اور شامل ہو والا اگر جمیع یا یصلح لہ کے لئے مستغرق نہیں ہے ۔ نجمع منگر تو وہ جمع منکر ہے۔ فالعام لفظ تو عام ایسے لفظ کا نام ہے جس کی وضع بوضع واحد کثیر معانی کے لئے ہو ایسے کثیر جو غیر محصور ہوں اور یہ لفظ جمیع ما یصلح لہ کو مستغرق اور شامل ہو وضعا واحدا عام کی تعریف میں قیود احتراز یہ کو واضح کیا جا رہا ہے یعنی وضع کے ساتھ جو واحد کی قید کی گئی ہے اس کے ساتھ مشترک کو خارج کرنا مقصود ہے کہ اس کے اندر وضع واحد نہیں ہوتی بلکہ اوضاع کثیرہ ہوتی ہیں۔ والكثير يخرج اور لفظ کثیر کے ساتھ اس کو خارج کیا گیا ہے جس کی وضع کثیر کے لئے نہیں ہے جیسے زید و عمر وغیرہ وغیر محصور اور اس قید کے ساتھ ان الفاظ کو خارج کیا کیا جن کی وضع کثیر کے لئے تو ہے لیکن وہ غیر محصور نہیں بلکہ محصور میں جیسے اسمام العدد مثلا لفظ مانہ اس کی وضع کثیر کے لئے بوضع واحد ہے اور جمیع ما یصلے لہ کے لئے مستغرق بھی ہے لیکن میں محصور اس لئے خارج از تعریف عام ہو جائیگا وقوله مستغرق جميع ما يصلح له یہ قید جمع منکر کو خارج کرتی ہے جیسے رایت رجالا یہی مراد ہے والا مجمع منکر کی یعنی اگر جمیع ما یصلح لہ کو مستغرق نہیں ہے تو وہ جمع منکر ہے وقوله نحوه یعنی نحو الجمع المنكر جیسے جماعۃ من الرجال یہ بھی عام کی تعریف سے خارج ہے کیو یہ بھی مستغرق نہیں ہے ۔ فعلى قول من لا تقول دراصل اس میں اختلاف ہے کہ جمع منکر میں عموم ہے یا نہیں ہے بعض حضرات جمع منکر میں عموم کے قائل نہیں ہیں مصنف کا نظریہ بھی ان کے موافق ہے تو ان حضرات کے نزدیک جمع منکر واسطہ ہوگی بین الخاص والعام نہ عام ہوگی نہ خاص بلکہ بین بین ہوگی وعلی قول من يقول بعمومه بعض حضرات جمع منکر میں عموم کے قائل ہیں اور اس کو عام میں داخل کرتے ہیں تو ان کے قول کے مطابق پھر مصنف کے قول الا فجمع منکر میں جمع منکر سے مراد وہ جمع منکر ہوگی جس کے ساتھ کوئی قرینہ موجود ہو کہ یہ عموم کے لئے نہیں تو پھر یہی جمع منکر واسطه بين العام والخاص ہوگی نحورائیت الیوم رجالا کیونکہ اس جگہ قرینہ موجود ہے کہ یہاں رجال سے عموم مراد نہیں ہے خان من المعلوم کیونکہ یہ یقینا معلوم ہے جمع رجال مرتی نہیں پھر علی الخصوصوں الیوم یعنی آج کے ایک دن میں تو اس قسم کی جمع منکر واسطہ ہوگی بین العام والخاص - دان کان ای الکثیر محصول اگر وہ کثیر معانی محصور میں کالعدد والتثنية او وضع للواحد یا اس لفظ کی وضع ایک معنی کے لئے ہے فخاصی یہ دو نو خاص ہیں ایک لفظ موضوع للكثير المحصور اور دوسرا موضوع للمعنى الواحد سواء كان الواحد باعتبار الشخص یعنی خواہ اس واحد میں وحدۃ شخصیہ ہو کہ اس کا صدق على الافراد الکثیر نہ ہو سکے کزید اور باعتبار النوع یعنی اس کے اندر وحدۃ نوعیہ ہو جو اس نوع کے افراد کثیرہ پر صادق آسکتی ہے جیسے رجل اور فرس یا اس کے اندر وحدۃ جنسیہ ہو جیسے انسان یہ تمام
The author said in al-Tawdih: The first division is according to the wad' (coining) of the word for meaning. So presenting the division, he said: The word, if it is coined for many meanings by multiple wad's, each meaning having a separate wad' – then that is mushtarak (homonym), like the word 'ayn: one wad' for the seeing eye, another for gold (dhahab), a third for 'ayn al-mizan (the pointer of the scale). Or (if it is coined) by one single wad' – i.e., the word is not coined for many meanings by multiple wad's but by one wad' for many meanings, and that many is unlimited. If these many meanings for which the word is coined by one wad' are unlimited, i.e., not confined within a limit and encompassment, then it is 'aam (general) provided that it encompasses all that which it is valid (to apply to). So if it does not encompass all that which it is valid to apply to, then it is jama' munkar (indefinite plural). Thus, 'aam is the name for a word whose wad' is by one wad' for many meanings, such many being unlimited, and this word encompasses all that which it is valid to apply to. By the restriction of 'wad'an wahidan', mushtarak is excluded because it has multiple wad's not one. By the restriction of 'al-kathir', what is not coined for many is excluded, like Zayd and 'Amr etc. By 'ghayri mahsur', words whose wad' is for many but limited are excluded, like the names of numbers, e.g., the word 'mi'a' (one hundred): its wad' is for many by one wad' and it encompasses all that it is valid for, but it is limited, so it is outside the definition of 'aam. By the phrase 'mustaghriqun li-jami'i ma yaslahu lahu', the indefinite plural is excluded, like 'ra'aytu rijalan' (I saw men). That is the meaning of 'jama' munkar'. And by 'nahwahu' (like it) is meant similar to the indefinite plural, like 'jama'atun min al-rijal' (a group of men): this is also outside the definition of 'aam because it is not encompassing. Now, according to the saying of those who do not hold that the indefinite plural has generality – in fact, there is disagreement about whether the indefinite plural has generality or not. Some scholars do not hold that the indefinite plural has generality; the author's view also agrees with them. So in their view, the indefinite plural is intermediate between khas and 'aam – neither 'aam nor khas but between the two. According to those who hold that it has generality, they include it in 'aam. Then in their view, the author's saying 'illa jama' munkar' means that indefinite plural which has a qarina (contextual indicator) that it is not for generality. Then this indefinite plural is intermediate between 'aam and khas, like 'ra'aytu al-yawma rijalan' (today I saw men) because here there is a qarina that 'rijal' does not mean generality; it is known that a group of men is not seen in one day. So this kind of indefinite plural is intermediate between 'aam and khas. Otherwise, if the many is limited – like numbers, dual – or (the word is) coined for a single meaning, then it is khas. These are two specific types: a word coined for limited many, and a word coined for a single meaning, whether the oneness is by way of person (like Zayd), or by way of species (like 'rajul' and 'faras'), or by way of genus (like 'insan'). All those are specific.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a definition of 'aam (general word) from the Hanafi usul perspective, expanding on the matn of al-Tawdih. The author begins by presenting the first division of words based on wad': if a word is coined for multiple meanings with separate coinings, it is mushtarak (homonym). If coined for many meanings by a single coining, it is either 'aam (if the many is unlimited and the word encompasses all individuals it can apply to) or jama' munkar (if it does not encompass all). The definition includes four restrictive clauses: (1) wad'an wahidan excludes mushtarak; (2) al-kathir excludes proper nouns like Zayd; (3) ghayri mahsur excludes limited plurals like numbers; (4) mustaghriqun li-jami'i ma yaslahu lahu excludes indefinite plurals like 'rijalan' which are not comprehensive. The passage then discusses a dispute about the status of jama' munkar: some scholars, including the author, hold that it is not general but intermediate; others consider it general unless a qarina indicates otherwise. Finally, it clarifies that words coined for limited many (like numbers) or for a single meaning (whether by person, species, or genus) are 'khas' (specific). This analysis is purely definitional and does not rely on scriptural proofs, though it sets the stage for later application of these categories to Quranic and hadith texts.

Key Terms
العام (al-'aam): A word coined by a single wad' for many unlimited meanings, encompassing all that it is valid to apply to.
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A word coined for multiple meanings with separate coinings for each meaning.
جمع منكر (jama' munkar): An indefinite plural that does not encompass all individuals; it is intermediate between 'aam and khas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following words does NOT qualify as 'aam (general) according to the definition in the passage?
A) "رجل" (rajul) because it is coined for a single type and is not comprehensive.
B) "الناس" (al-nas) because it is coined for all people.
C) "مائة" (mi'a) because it is coined for a limited number and is thus mahsur.
D) "رجال" (rijal) in the phrase "رأيت رجالا" (ra'aytu rijalan) because it is an indefinite plural.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of 'aam requires four conditions: single wad', many, unlimited, and comprehensive — without these, the word is not 'aam.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Classification of Lafz by Wad': 'Amm, Khass, Mushtarak, Mu'awwal
Defines and classifies 'amm and related terms based on wad'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any Arabic word according to its wad' (linguistic assignment) and identify whether it is 'amm, khass, mushtarak, or mu'awwal.

Core Idea
A word's legal scope depends on its linguistic assignment: single or multiple, limited or unlimited.

Before we can derive rulings from texts, we must understand the linguistic categories that govern how words convey meaning. This passage presents the Hanafi taxonomy of lafz based on wad' — the foundational tool for determining whether a word is general, specific, ambiguous, or interpreted. Mastering this classification is your first step in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main branches
Start with the big picture: a word is either assigned for one meaning or for many meanings. That is the first fork in the classification tree.
2
Analyze the 'many meanings' branch
If a word is assigned for many meanings, check whether the assignment is one (wad' wahid) or multiple (wad' kathir). Multiple assignments = mushtarak. Single assignment: then check if the many are limited (like numbers) or unlimited.
Limited many = khass; unlimited leads to next step.
3
Distinguish 'amm from indefinite plural
For unlimited many with single wad', test whether the word encompasses (istighraq) all individuals it can apply to. If yes, it is 'amm. If not, it is jam' munakkar (indefinite plural) — an intermediate category.
This is a key Hanafi distinction; most students confuse indefinite plurals with 'amm.
4
Identify khass subtypes
Khass includes both words for a single meaning (individual, type, genus) and words for limited multiplicities (numbers, duals). Recognize that 'khass' is broader than just singulars.
5
Understand mu'awwal's place
Mu'awwal is not a division based on wad' but on ijtihad: a mushtarak meaning preferred by evidence. It is a result of scholarly effort, not a linguistic category.
Mu'awwal is included in the fourfold division but is separate from the wad'-based tree.
