ہوگا اور نہ جزی بلکہ اس کے اجزاء میں سے ایک جزء ہوگا۔
Fourthly: that the placement (wad') is for a multitude from a single placer, whether a single person like the language placer or a single type like the terminology of a specific group; then the mentioned words are not placed for a multitude from a single placer, so no objection. His statement: 'al-'aam is a word placed with a single placement' means that 'aam is called a word whose placement is for multiple non-restricted meanings with a single placement, and it encompasses all that which is suitable for it. So his statement 'a single placement' means that with the restriction of a single placement, the mushtarak (homonym) is excluded - that is, mushtarak with respect to multiple meanings is outside the definition, because it has multiple placements, not a single placement. But with respect to individuals of a single meaning - that is, when one meaning of the mushtarak is considered, it includes all individuals of that meaning; from this perspective it can be called 'aam, and it is included under the limit, because at that time it includes a non-restricted multitude with a single placement, like the word 'ayn meaning a flowing spring includes all individuals of springs; from this aspect it is general. And the closer and better view is that the restriction of a single placement should not be made for exclusion, but rather for investigation of the place and clarification of intent regarding mushtarak, because mushtarak with respect to its multiple meanings is not separate, as will come in the discussion of mushtarak that there is no generality in mushtarak; therefore, the restriction of istighraq (inclusivity) is sufficient for excluding mushtarak. In response it is said: when each of two restrictions can serve as an excluder, then attributing exclusion to the first restriction is more appropriate than attributing it to the second. If it is said that in the form of the question the commentator's mentioned objection - that mushtarak is excluded by the restriction of istighraq, not by the single placement - the answer is that the summary is that istighraq means generality, whether istighraq 'ala sabil al-shumul (simultaneous inclusion) such that it includes all at once like the plural forms e.g. 'rijal' and 'qawm', or istighraq 'ala sabil al-badal (alternate inclusion) such that it includes one then leaves it and includes another, like 'Whoever enters my house, then for him is such-and-such' - it can include each person on an alternate basis, whether Zayd, Amr, or Bakr, no specification, but each can be. So mushtarak with respect to all its meanings can be inclusive on an alternate basis, though not on a simultaneous basis; therefore the restriction of istighraq cannot be an excluder. We say: 'namin'? The summary of the answer is that this generalization within istighraq is not correct, otherwise the positive indefinite noun (nakerah muthbatah), which is not general, would be included in 'aam because it is also inclusive of all that which suits it on an alternate basis - that is, it includes every individual on an alternate basis. Earlier, this is a refutation of the commentator's 'we say' that although nakerah muthbatah is inclusive on an alternate basis, its placement is not for a multitude, therefore this restriction 'kathir' will exclude it from the definition of 'aam. We say: 'law sallam' - we do not first concede that its placement is not for a multitude; and even if we concede, your answer can only apply to singular nakerah, not to plural nakerah (nakirah jam'): because its placement is for a multitude, and it is inclusive of all individuals on an alternate basis, even those who do not accept generality of nakirah jam' concede that; so this nakirah jam' would be included in 'aam, whereas according to those who deny generality it is not general. And the intended meaning of 'al-wad' li al-katheer' is 'al-wad' li kulli wahid'. Since the author included mushtarak, 'aam, and asma' al-'adad within 'wad' li al-katheer', how can all of these be included under 'wad' li al-katheer' when asma' al-'adad are not placed for multiple meanings but for a single meaning - for example, 'thalatha' is placed for the number three, and 'mi'ah' for one hundred; these are for a single meaning, i.e., a specific number. So the commentator explained so that all these matters are included under 'wad' li al-katheer'. The summary is that in 'wad' li al-katheer', the preposition 'li' is not the preposition of the object of 'wad'' meaning that it was placed for multiple meanings, but rather it is 'li al-'aqibah' and 'al-sayrurah' meaning that the word was placed so that multiple meanings may be obtained, whether its placement is for each one of the multiple individuals - like mushtarak - or its placement is for something that contains multiple individuals - like ma'arif with lam and kull afradi - or its placement is for a sum of individuals qua sum - like ism al-'adad e.g. 'mi'ah' is placed for a sum of one hundred units qua sum, and similarly a sum that contains generality and such a collective whole, these are also included in 'aam. So 'fayakunu kullu wahid' etc. This is a branching (tafri') upon the three mentioned branches in the manner of lay-and-fold (al-laf wa al-nashr). That is, with respect to the first branch, each individual of the multiple individuals is the very object of placement (ayn al-mawdhu' lahu). Mushtarak is included in this branch. 'In juzi'a': or each individual is not the very object of placement but rather one part (juz') of the parts of the object of placement, like ma'arif with lam and kull afradi: each individual is a part of the mawdhu' lahu - this is the second branch in which 'aam is included. 'Aw juz' min ajza'ihi': that is, each individual is neither the very object nor a part thereof, but rather one component (juz') from its components.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the definition of 'aam (general term) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. It opens by continuing an earlier point that the wad' (linguistic coinage) must be from a single clear origin. The text then refines the definition of 'aam: it is a word coined with a single act of placement (wad'an wahidan) for a non-restricted multitude of meanings, and it encompasses all that it can apply to. The restriction of single placement (wad' wahid) is intended to exclude mushtarak (homonymy), because mushtarak is coined for multiple distinct meanings each with its own placement. However, the commentator notes that if we consider mushtarak with respect to one meaning, that meaning may be general for its individuals (e.g., 'ayn as 'spring' includes all springs). The preferred view is that the condition of 'single placement' is not for exclusion but to clarify that mushtarak has no general coverage across its multiple meanings. Next, an objection is raised: why not exclude mushtarak simply by the condition of istighraq (inclusivity) instead of single placement? The answer distinguishes two types of istighraq: simultaneous (shumuli) and alternate (badali). Mushtarak can have alternate inclusivity (each meaning individually considered includes its individuals) but not simultaneous, so istighraq alone does not exclude it. The text then considers whether the positive indefinite noun (nakerah muthbatah) could be considered 'aam under the alternate inclusivity definition, but it is excluded because its placement is not for a multitude. The author further explains that 'wad' li al-katheer' in the definition is interpreted broadly: the 'li' indicates consequence, not intended purpose; the word is coined so that multiple individuals may be referred to, whether each individual is directly the object of placement (mushtarak), or each individual is a part of the object (like definite article with lam and kull afradi for 'aam), or each individual is a component of a sum (like numeral 'mi'ah' meaning a set of one hundred). Hanafi usul here shows its characteristic analytical depth, carefully differentiating between categories that might blur in simpler definitions.

Key Terms
العام (al-'aam): A word coined with a single placement for a non-restricted multitude, encompassing all its suitable referents.
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A word coined for multiple distinct meanings with separate placements for each.
الاستغراق (al-istighraq): Inclusivity of the word over all individuals, either simultaneously (shumuli) or alternately (badali).
النكرة المثبتة (al-nakirah al-muthbatah): A positive indefinite noun; in context, it is not considered 'aam because its placement is not for a multitude, even though it can have alternate inclusivity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition discussed, why is mushtarak (homonym) excluded from being 'aam with respect to its multiple meanings?
A) Because it does not have istighraq at all.
B) Because it is coined with multiple placements, not a single placement.
C) Because it can never be general even for one meaning.
D) Because its meanings are restricted.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of 'aam requires both a single linguistic coinage for a multitude and inclusivity over all suitable referents; mushtarak and indefinite nouns are excluded based on these conditions.

The Three Categories of Wad' li al-Kathir
Defines wad' li al-kathir and its three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three categories of wad' li al-kathir and classify given word types into the appropriate category using the commentator's criteria.

Core Idea
Wad' li al-kathir includes homonyms, general words, and numerals, each fitting a distinct category.

In this passage, the commentator expands on the definition of wad' li al-kathir, clarifying that it is not limited to personal coinage. He divides it into three categories: words coined for each unit, words coined for a collective, and words coined for a totality. Understanding this classification is essential for correctly identifying general ('am) expressions in the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition of wad' li al-kathir
Find where the commentator states that wad' li al-kathir means the word is coined such that it leads to multiplicity, whether through personal or generic coinage.
The preposition 'lam' in 'li al-kathir' indicates consequence, not direct object.
2
Distinguish personal vs. generic wad'
Note the distinction: personal wad' (wad' shakhsi) is for a specific meaning, while generic wad' (wad' naw'i) is a rule applicable to many words.
This distinction allows the indefinite noun in negative contexts to be included.
3
List the three categories with examples
Category 1: each unit is the exact subject matter (mushtarak). Category 2: each unit is a part of the subject matter (e.g., al-rijal). Category 3: each unit is a part of parts (e.g., numerals like mi'ah).
The general ('am) straddles categories 2 and 3.
4
Apply the third category to exclude proper nouns
Understand why words like Zayd do not belong: their parts are not meaningful units that fit the pattern of being 'one of the set'.
The condition is that the parts must be analogous to individuals of a species.
5
Recognize the two types of general within the scheme
Some general terms fall under category 2 (like al-rijal) and some under category 3 (like aggregates).
This shows the flexibility of the Hanafi concept of 'am.

This also encompasses all that is suitable for it (the general) in a substitutive manner, meaning it includes every individual on a substitutional basis. [Khayr?] Previously this is a refutation of the commentator's 'qulna' that although the positive indefinite noun on a substitutional basis is comprehensive, its wad' (linguistic coinage) is not for multiplicity, so this restriction (kathir) removes it from the definition of the general. Qulna: law sallamna (we do not concede that its wad' is not for multiplicity). Even if we concede, your reply can only pertain to the singular indefinite noun, not to the plural of the indefinite (jam' al-munkar), because its wad' is for multiplicity and it encompasses all individuals on a substitutional basis. Even those who do not believe in the generality of the plural indefinite concede this, so this plural indefinite would be included in the general, although according to those who deny generality it is not general. And the meaning of 'wad' li al-kathir' is 'wad' li kulli wahid' (wad' for each one). Since the author included mushtarak (homonym), general, and numerals under 'wad' li al-kathir', how can all these be included in 'wad' li al-ma'ani al-kathirah' when numerals are not coined for multiple meanings but for one meaning? For example, thulathah is coined for the number three, and mi'ah for one hundred; this is a single meaning, i.e., a specific number. So the commentator provided an explanation (tawjih) so that all these matters are included under 'wad' li al-kathir'. The summary is that in 'wad' li al-kathir', the preposition 'lam' is not the connective of the verbal noun (wad') and its object, which would mean that it is coined for multiple meanings, but rather the 'lam' is for consequence (ta'qibah) and resultant state (sayrurah). So the meaning is that the word is coined such that multiple meanings come about, whether its wad' is for each one of multiple units, like mushtarak; or its wad' is for a single entity that includes multiple units, like the definite with 'al' and 'kull al-afradi' (each individual is a part of the subject matter) - the general falls under this second category; or it is a part of its parts, meaning each unit is neither the exact subject matter nor a part of it but a part of its parts. Under this third category falls numerals, like mi'ah, each unit is a part of the aggregate, and similarly the general aggregate (majmu') and the general collective (kull majmu'i) also fall under this third category. The mushtarak is included in the first category, the general (the definite with 'al' and 'kull al-afradi) in the second, and numerals in the third. Part of the general (the aggregate and collective general) also falls in the third category. So some of the general falls in the second category and some in the third. If it is said: then like Zayd, etc., would also be included, because the generalization (ta'mim) within 'wad' li al-kathir' would then also include Zayd and rajul and faras, etc., for they have multiplicity in terms of parts and limbs, so they would fall in the third category. Qulna: The consideration is that the parts that matter in the multiplicity of parts are those that are agreed upon in the name. For example, mi'ah (one hundred): its parts are individual units, each unit is called by its name and each unit is said to be 'one of the hundred'. In that respect, these parts become suitable for particular instances (juz'iyyat), just as each individual human is said to be 'one of mankind'. Similarly, each unit of mi'ah is said to be 'one of the mi'ah'. Thus, the single meaning that is one in terms of the essential constituents (mutahid bi hasab al-muqawwam) has a proportionality (tanasub) between its instances and these parts. Unlike Zayd and Shimr, for they do not have this kind of multiplicity of parts, so they are excluded from 'wad' li al-kathir'. [Khayr?] The objection is: the indefinite noun in a negative context (nakira manfiya) is called 'am (general) by usul scholars, yet its wad' is not for multiplicity, so how can it be included in the general when wad' li al-kathir is necessary for generality? Qulna: Wad' is more general than personal (shakhsi) wad'. Personal wad' is like when a word is coined for a specific meaning, like Zayd for the particular person, or darb for the specific meaning of beating. And generic (naw'i) wad' is where no specific word is coined; rather, this wad' takes the form of a general rule, like it is said: every word that is on the pattern of 'fa'il' is for an entity in which the meaning of the action resides. Here, our intended meaning of wad' is general, whether personal or generic. So the wad' of the negative indefinite for multiplicity is a generic wad'. And it is established from their usage that the negative indefinite (nakira manfiya) - the derivation of a rule for generic wad' is being made. Commentary
This passage is a commentary expansion on the definition of 'wad' li al-kathir' (linguistic coinage for multiplicity) and its inclusion of various word types. The commentator first addresses an objection from an opponent (likely one who restricts the definition of 'am to words coined for multiplicity) regarding the indefinite positive noun (nakira muthbata) which is comprehensive but not coined for multiplicity. The commentator refutes by saying that even if conceded, the objection fails for the plural indefinite and also explains that the indefinite positive noun does have a generic wad' for multiplicity. Then the commentator analyzes the three categories (shiqaq) of wad' li al-kathir: (1) words coined for each individual unit of multiple meanings, like mushtarak (homonyms); (2) words coined for a single entity that encompasses many units, like the definite with 'al' and 'kull al-afradi', which covers the general ('am); (3) words coined for a totality of parts, like numerals (e.g., mi'ah) and collective terms (majmu' and kull majmu'i). The passage clarifies that the general is partly in the second category (e.g., al-rijal) and partly in the third (e.g., the aggregate general like al-qawm). The commentator then responds to a hypothetical objection about why proper nouns like Zayd and common nouns like rajul are not included, explaining that the necessary multiplicity of parts must be andād (equivalent) and compositionally related to the species, which is not true for Zayd. Finally, the commentator addresses the nakira manfiya (indefinite in a negative context), which usul scholars treat as 'am despite not having a personal wad' for multiplicity. The commentator distinguishes between personal wad' (wad' shakhsi) and generic wad' (wad' naw'i), asserting that the nakira manfiya has a generic wad' for multiplicity, established by inductive inference from usage.

Key Terms
وضع للكثير (wad' li al-kathir): A word coined in such a way that it leads to multiplicity of meanings or referents, either by being coined for each unit of many (mushtarak), for a single entity containing many (like 'al-'am), or for a totality of parts (like numerals).
وضع شخصی (wad' shakhsi): Personal coinage: when a specific word is coined for a specific meaning (e.g., زيد for a person).
وضع نوعی (wad' naw'i): Generic coinage: a rule or pattern of coinage (e.g., every word on pattern فاعل is for the doer); not tied to a specific word.
عام ('am): A word that encompasses all its referents comprehensively (isti'ghraq).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under which category of wad' li al-kathir does the word 'مائة' (hundred) fall?
A) The first category (each unit is the exact subject matter).
B) The second category (each unit is a part of the subject matter).
C) The third category (each unit is a part of parts).
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
Wad' li al-kathir comprises three categories: coinage per unit (mushtarak), per collective (general with 'al'), and per aggregate (numerals), with generic coinage sufficient for inclusion.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
6 min
Generic Coinage and the Nakira Manfiya Dispute
Addresses the dispute over the generality of the indefinite noun in negative contexts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers the indefinite noun in a negative context (nakira manfiya) as 'am (general) by applying the concept of generic wad' (wad' naw'i).

Core Idea
The nakira manfiya is 'am because it has generic wad' for multiplicity, even without personal coinage.

The passage raises a key dispute: opponents argue that the indefinite noun in a negative context cannot be 'am because it lacks wad' li al-kathir. The commentator responds by distinguishing personal coinage from generic coinage, arguing that the nakira manfiya falls under the latter. This distinction broadens the scope of general expressions in Hanafi usul and has significant implications for interpreting Quranic verses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's objection
Find where the objector states that nakira manfiya is not coined for multiplicity, so it cannot be 'am.
The objection is based on a narrow definition of wad' li al-kathir as personal coinage.
2
Understand the Hanafi response: generic wad'
The commentator argues that 'wad'' is broader, including both personal (shakhsi) and generic (naw'i) coinage. Give examples.
Generic wad' is derived from a rule, e.g., 'fa'il' pattern indicates the doer.
3
Apply generic wad' to the nakira manfiya
Explain how the nakira manfiya (e.g., 'ما جاءني رجل') obtains a generic wad' from its usage in negative contexts, leading to universality.
This is based on inductive evidence from the Arabic language.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah)
In a negative sentence like 'ما جاءني رجل', the Hanafi interpretation is 'no man came to me' (general), while opponents may not affirm generality.
This affects legal rulings derived from such expressions.

يندرج فيه المشترك باعتبار الشق الاول والعام الى المعرف باللام والكل الافرادي يندرج فيه باعتبار الشق الثاني واسماء العدد يندرج باعتبار الشق الثالث ومن العام المجموع والكل المجموعى ايضا يندرج فى هذا الشق الثالث فالعام بعضه اندرج في الشق الثاني وبعضه اندرج في الشق اللافت فان قيل فيندرج فيه مثل زيد الخ وضع للكثیر کے اندر جو تعمیم کی ہے یہ اسی پر اعتراض ہے خلاصہ یہ کہ پھر تو زید اور رجل وفرس وغیرہ بھی اس تعمیم کے لحاظ سے وضع للکثیر میں داخل ہو جائیں گے کیونکہ ان کے اندر کثرة بحسب الاجزاء والاعضاء موجود ہے تو شق ثالث میں درج ہو جائیگا قلنا المعتبر خلاصہ جواب یہ کہ کثیر سبب الاجزاء میں وہ اجزاء معتبر ہیں جو متفق فی الاسم ہوں کا حاد المائة کہ اس کے اجزا یک صدا کائیاں ہیں اور ہر ایک اکائی کے نام سے موسوم ہے اور ہر ایک اکائی پر صادق آتا ہے کہ انہ واحد من المائہ تو اس لحاظ سے فانها تناسب جزئيات الم یعنی اس وقت یہ اجزاء مناسب جزئیات ہو جائیں گی جیسے کہ ہر فرد انسان پر صادق آتا ہے کہ انہ واحد من الانسان اسی طرح سرا کائی پر صادق آتا ہے کہ انہ واحد من المانہ تو اس اعتبار سے معنی واحد جو کہ متحد بحسب المقوم ہے اس کے جزئیات کے ساتھ ان اجزا کو تناسب حاصل ہو جائیگا بخلاف زید و شمر کے کہ ان کے اندر اس قسم کی کثرۃ اجزا نہیں ہے لہذا وہ کثیر المعنی سے خارج ہو گا خان قیل حاصل یہ ہے کہ نکرہ منفیہ کو اصولی حضرات عام کہتے ہیں حالانکہ اس کی وضع کثیر کے لئے نہیں ہے تو یہ عام میں کیسے درج ہو سکتا ہے جب کہ عام میں وضع للكثیر ضروری ہے ۔ قلنا الوضع اعم من الشخصی شخصی وضع جیسے ایک لفظ کی مخصوص معنی کے لئے وضع ہو جیسے زید کی برائے مسمی مخصوص اور ضرب کی برائے زدن جو معنی مخصوص ہے والنوعی نوعی وضع کے اندر کوئی لفظ مخصوص نہیں ہوتا بلکہ یہ وضع ایک قاعدہ کلیہ کی صورت میں ہوتی ہے یسے کہا جاتا ہے کہ ہر وہ لفظ جو علی وزن فاعل ہو تو وہ ایسی ذات کے لئے ہوگا جس کے ساتھ معنی فعل قائم ہو تو یہاں ہماری مراد وضع سے عام ہے خواہ شخصی ہو یا نوعی تو نکرہ منفیہ کی وضع کثیر کے لئے وضع نوعی ہے ۔ وقد ثبت من استعمالهم نکرہ منفیہ کے اندر وضع نوعی شنا کرنے کے لئے استنباط قاعدہ کیا جارہا ہے خلاصہ یہ کہ علماء حضرات جو نکرہ منفیہ کی استعمال
This also encompasses all that is suitable for it (the general) in a substitutive manner, meaning it includes every individual on a substitutional basis. [Khayr?] Previously this is a refutation of the commentator's 'qulna' that although the positive indefinite noun on a substitutional basis is comprehensive, its wad' (linguistic coinage) is not for multiplicity, so this restriction (kathir) removes it from the definition of the general. Qulna: law sallamna (we do not concede that its wad' is not for multiplicity). Even if we concede, your reply can only pertain to the singular indefinite noun, not to the plural of the indefinite (jam' al-munkar), because its wad' is for multiplicity and it encompasses all individuals on a substitutional basis. Even those who do not believe in the generality of the plural indefinite concede this, so this plural indefinite would be included in the general, although according to those who deny generality it is not general. And the meaning of 'wad' li al-kathir' is 'wad' li kulli wahid' (wad' for each one). Since the author included mushtarak (homonym), general, and numerals under 'wad' li al-kathir', how can all these be included in 'wad' li al-ma'ani al-kathirah' when numerals are not coined for multiple meanings but for one meaning? For example, thulathah is coined for the number three, and mi'ah for one hundred; this is a single meaning, i.e., a specific number. So the commentator provided an explanation (tawjih) so that all these matters are included under 'wad' li al-kathir'. The summary is that in 'wad' li al-kathir', the preposition 'lam' is not the connective of the verbal noun (wad') and its object, which would mean that it is coined for multiple meanings, but rather the 'lam' is for consequence (ta'qibah) and resultant state (sayrurah). So the meaning is that the word is coined such that multiple meanings come about, whether its wad' is for each one of multiple units, like mushtarak; or its wad' is for a single entity that includes multiple units, like the definite with 'al' and 'kull al-afradi' (each individual is a part of the subject matter) - the general falls under this second category; or it is a part of its parts, meaning each unit is neither the exact subject matter nor a part of it but a part of its parts. Under this third category falls numerals, like mi'ah, each unit is a part of the aggregate, and similarly the general aggregate (majmu') and the general collective (kull majmu'i) also fall under this third category. The mushtarak is included in the first category, the general (the definite with 'al' and 'kull al-afradi) in the second, and numerals in the third. Part of the general (the aggregate and collective general) also falls in the third category. So some of the general falls in the second category and some in the third. If it is said: then like Zayd, etc., would also be included, because the generalization (ta'mim) within 'wad' li al-kathir' would then also include Zayd and rajul and faras, etc., for they have multiplicity in terms of parts and limbs, so they would fall in the third category. Qulna: The consideration is that the parts that matter in the multiplicity of parts are those that are agreed upon in the name. For example, mi'ah (one hundred): its parts are individual units, each unit is called by its name and each unit is said to be 'one of the hundred'. In that respect, these parts become suitable for particular instances (juz'iyyat), just as each individual human is said to be 'one of mankind'. Similarly, each unit of mi'ah is said to be 'one of the mi'ah'. Thus, the single meaning that is one in terms of the essential constituents (mutahid bi hasab al-muqawwam) has a proportionality (tanasub) between its instances and these parts. Unlike Zayd and Shimr, for they do not have this kind of multiplicity of parts, so they are excluded from 'wad' li al-kathir'. [Khayr?] The objection is: the indefinite noun in a negative context (nakira manfiya) is called 'am (general) by usul scholars, yet its wad' is not for multiplicity, so how can it be included in the general when wad' li al-kathir is necessary for generality? Qulna: Wad' is more general than personal (shakhsi) wad'. Personal wad' is like when a word is coined for a specific meaning, like Zayd for the particular person, or darb for the specific meaning of beating. And generic (naw'i) wad' is where no specific word is coined; rather, this wad' takes the form of a general rule, like it is said: every word that is on the pattern of 'fa'il' is for an entity in which the meaning of the action resides. Here, our intended meaning of wad' is general, whether personal or generic. So the wad' of the negative indefinite for multiplicity is a generic wad'. And it is established from their usage that the negative indefinite (nakira manfiya) - the derivation of a rule for generic wad' is being made. The summary is that scholars who use the negative indefinite...
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expansion on the definition of 'wad' li al-kathir' (linguistic coinage for multiplicity) and its inclusion of various word types. The commentator first addresses an objection from an opponent (likely one who restricts the definition of 'am to words coined for multiplicity) regarding the indefinite positive noun (nakira muthbata) which is comprehensive but not coined for multiplicity. The commentator refutes by saying that even if conceded, the objection fails for the plural indefinite and also explains that the indefinite positive noun does have a generic wad' for multiplicity. Then the commentator analyzes the three categories (shiqaq) of wad' li al-kathir: (1) words coined for each individual unit of multiple meanings, like mushtarak (homonyms); (2) words coined for a single entity that encompasses many units, like the definite with 'al' and 'kull al-afradi', which covers the general ('am); (3) words coined for a totality of parts, like numerals (e.g., mi'ah) and collective terms (majmu' and kull majmu'i). The passage clarifies that the general is partly in the second category (e.g., al-rijal) and partly in the third (e.g., the aggregate general like al-qawm). The commentator then responds to a hypothetical objection about why proper nouns like Zayd and common nouns like rajul are not included, explaining that the necessary multiplicity of parts must be andād (equivalent) and compositionally related to the species, which is not true for Zayd. Finally, the commentator addresses the nakira manfiya (indefinite in a negative context), which usul scholars treat as 'am despite not having a personal wad' for multiplicity. The commentator distinguishes between personal wad' (wad' shakhsi) and generic wad' (wad' naw'i), asserting that the nakira manfiya has a generic wad' for multiplicity, established by inductive inference from usage.

Key Terms
نكرة منفية (nakira manfiya): An indefinite noun placed in a negative context, used to express complete negation (e.g., 'ما جاءني رجل' = no man came).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the commentator refute the objection that the nakira manfiya is not general?
A) By claiming it has personal wad' for each individual.
B) By distinguishing personal from generic wad' and asserting that the nakira manfiya has generic wad'.
C) By arguing that the objection only applies to the plural indefinite.
D) By citing a Quranic verse.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school includes the indefinite noun in negative contexts under 'am by invoking generic coinage (wad' naw'i), broadening the scope of general expressions.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
Generality of Negated Indefinite and the Condition of Non-Enumerated Many
Analyzing the linguistic and rational basis for the generality of negated indefinite and the condition of non-enumerated many.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the negated indefinite indicates real generality, and evaluate the condition of non-enumerated many in identifying general terms.

Core Idea
Negated indefinite is a real general, not metaphorical; and a general term must apply to a non-enumerated many.

In this lesson, we delve into two key aspects of 'amm (general) in Hanafi usul: the status of the negated indefinite (naqira manfiya) and the qualifier of non-enumerated many (kathir ghayr mahsur). The Hanafi school asserts that the negated indefinite indicates generality as a reality (haqiqah), supported by rational necessity and generic convention. Additionally, the definition of 'amm requires that its referent be non-enumerated, excluding number nouns. We will follow the author's argument, his refutation of objections, and the proof from consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Understand that the main claim is that the negated indefinite indicates generality of negation—every individual of that indefinite is negated.
2
Understand the Hanafi proof
The indefinite is used in its original meaning for a vague individual. Generality follows from the logical nature of negation: negation of a vague individual cannot occur without negating all individuals.
3
Recognize the opposing view and Hanafi response
Some scholars claim the generality is metaphorical. The Hanafi response invokes generic convention (wad' naw'i): the coiner rationally established this rule for all indefinites in negation.
The muhaqqiqun of Ibn Hajib's Usul confirm the Hanafi view.
4
Analyze the condition of non-enumerated many
In the definition of 'amm, the qualifier 'non-enumerated many' means the word does not indicate a specific number. This excludes number nouns but includes plurals and collectives.
5
Evaluate objections and responses
Objection: The qualifier is redundant because 'istighraq lima yaslahu lahu' already excludes numbers. Response: Number nouns do indicate individuals by implicational indication (dalalah tadminiyyah), so they would enter the definition without the qualifier.
The passage addresses this with the example of 'al-mi'a' (hundred).

نہیں رہتا حالانکہ یہ بالا تفاق عام ہے اور دوسری قباحت یہ ہے کہ اس معنی کے لحاظ لفظ الف الف كثير عغیر محصور کے لئے موضوع ہو کیونکہ اہل عرب أمة أمية " تھی جو حساب و کتاب نہیں جانتے تھے تو اتنا بڑا عدد ان کی شمار میں نہیں آسکتا تھا تو پھر یہ عام ہو جائے حالانکہ یہ اسم عدد ہے جو عام نہیں بلکہ عام کا قسیم تھا ۔ لایقال هذا القيد مستدرک یعنی غیر محصور والى
قید نداند از ضرورت اور فضول ہے کیونکہ اسی قید کے ساتھ عدد سے احتراز کرنا مقصود - حالانکہ عدد استغراق لما یصلح لہ کی قید سے خارج ہو جائیگا کیونکہ اسم عد مثل لفظ المائتہ یہ مانت کی جزئیات پر تو صادق آنے کی صلاحیت رکھتا ہے یعنی سیکڑے سیکڑے پر صادق آنے کی صلاحیت رکھتا ہے اور ان احاد واکائیوں پر صادق آنیکی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتا جو اس عدد کے ضمن میں میں کیونکہ لفظ مائتہ ایک ایک اکائی پر صادق آنے کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتا اس لیئے استغراق لما يصلح لہ کے قید کے ساتھ اسم عدد خارج از عام ہو جائیگا لہذا لفظ غیر محصور کی ضروت نہیں ہے لانا نقول سے اس کو رد کر دیا ہے اور کہا اراد بالصلوح یعنی صلاحیت سے مراد اس قسم کی صلاحیت ہے جیسے اسم کلی اپنی جزئیات پر صادق آنے کی صلاحیت رکھتا ہے۔ او الكل لاجزائے یا جیسے کل اپنے اجزاء پر صادق آتا ہے اب کل ایک ایک جز پر تو محمول نہیں ہوتا بلکہ مجموعہ اجزاء کو کل کہا جاتا ہے۔ فاعتبر الدلالة یعنی یہ لفظ جن کے لئے صلاحیت رکھتا ہے ان پر دال ہو خواہ بدلالہ مطابقیہ یا تضمینہ تو لفظ مائتہ میں صلاحیت کل للاجزاء والی اور : دلالت بدلالة تضمینہ علی الاجزا موجود ہے ۔
وبهذا الاعتبار صارت صبغ الجموع اور اسی اعتبار کے لحاظ سے صیغ جموع جیسے المسلمون والرجال اور اسمار جموع جیسے رحط وقوم اپنے افراد کی بنسبت مستغرق لجميع ما يصلح له ہیں کیونکہ ان کے اندر بھی بنسبت احاد کے صلاحیت وہی صلاحیت صدق الكل على الاجزا کی ہے اور دلالتہ بھی تضمینہ ہے تو اس لحاظ سے مداخلت ای العدد تو اس صورت میں عدد
14. They say: This proves that the ruling of negation is for the many that is non-enumerated, and the word of negated indefinite includes each individual separately and collectively in this ruling of negation, i.e., negated indefinite indicates generality of negation. If the word is singular, then negation will include every single one of that singular; if the word is plural, then negation will include every totality generally. And negated indefinite does not indicate negation of generality called 'salb mashmul', i.e., it is not intended that its ruling does not include all, i.e., it includes some and not others; this meaning is not the meaning of negated indefinite. From this usage, it is established that indeed negated indefinite indicates generality of negation, and this is the generic convention (wad' naw'i). And being general: because the convention of the indefinite is for a vague individual, and reason says that negation of a vague individual cannot occur without every individual being negated, so this generality of negation in the indefinite is established with rational necessity, so it is not conventional but rational. The answer: This is not contrary to generic convention, because the generic convention under a universal rule is that the rule is derived rationally, so this is not inconsistent with being included in generic convention. Do not say: The negated indefinite is metaphorical (majaz), i.e., its generality is metaphorical, and the definition given is for real general, so the negated indefinite which is metaphorical general is not included in it. To this we say: We do not accept that it is metaphorical. How can it be said metaphorical when the usage of the negated indefinite is in its specific conventionally assigned meaning (wad' shakhsi), which is a vague individual—i.e., from the perspective of convention, a vague individual is its assigned meaning, and the generality is derived from occurrence under negation. So when it is used in its specific conventional meaning, it is reality (haqiqah). However, its generality is derived from the fact that negation of a vague individual cannot occur except through the negation of every individual. So it is as if the coiner said: Every indefinite that occurs under negation is for the negation of every individual. So from the perspective of this rule, it is generic convention (wad' naw'i). And the esteemed scholars have clearly stated in the commentary of Ibn Hajib's Usul that the usage of negated indefinite for generality is based on reality, so it cannot be called metaphorical. And the meaning of 'many being non-enumerated' is that the word does not indicate being confined to a specific number. Otherwise, all many things in existence are actually confined and finite, but because the word does not indicate being confined to a specific number, it is general (amm), encompassing non-enumerated many. Do not say: What is meant by non-enumerated is another meaning—the proponent of 'non-enumerated' gives a second meaning; the conclusion is that 'non-enumerated' means things that are not subject to enumeration and counting; it is called non-enumerated. To this we respond: If this meaning is intended, then the word 'al-samawat' (the heavens) would be specific because the heavens are only seven as the Quran states, so then the word 'al-samawat' would not be general, yet it is unanimously general. Another flaw: according to this meaning, the word 'alf' (thousand) would be for a non-enumerated many, because the Arabs were an illiterate nation who did not know calculation, so such a large number could not be counted by them; then it would become general, whereas it is a number noun which is not general but opposed to general. Do not say: This qualifier 'ghayr mahsur' is superfluous and unnecessary because the same purpose of excluding numbers is achieved by the qualifier 'istighraq lima yaslahu lahu' (comprehensiveness of that which it is suitable for), since number nouns like 'al-mi'a' (hundred) are suitable to apply to its particulars (hundreds) but not to each unit that constitutes that number, so with the qualifier 'istighraq lima yaslahu lahu', number nouns are excluded from general, thus the word 'ghayr mahsur' is not needed. To this we respond: He intended by 'suitability' (salahiyyah) the type of suitability like a universal noun suitable to apply to its particulars, or 'kull' (whole) suitable for its parts. Now 'kull' is not predicated on each individual part but on the totality of parts. So he considered the indication (dalalah): the word indicates that which it is suitable for, whether by direct indication (dalalah mutabaqiyyah) or by implicational indication (dalalah tadminiyyah). In the word 'mi'a' there exists suitability of the whole for its parts and indication by implicational indication upon the parts. And by this consideration, forms of plurals like 'al-muslimun', 'al-rijal', and singular collective nouns like 'rahbat' and 'qawm' are comprehensive of all that they are suitable for in relation to their individuals, because in them also, in relation to individuals, there is the same suitability of the whole applying to the parts, and their indication is also implicational. So from this perspective, numbers enter into the definition of general? Then in that case, numbers...
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the generality of the negated indefinite (naqira manfiya) and the condition of 'non-enumerated many' (kathir ghayr mahsur) in the definition of general ('amm). The author begins by establishing that the negated indefinite indicates generality of negation; that is, when an indefinite word is used in the context of negation, it negates every individual of that indefinite. This is proven by linguistic usage and rational necessity. The author refutes the claim that this generality is metaphorical, citing that the indefinite is used in its original sense for a vague individual, and the generality arises from the nature of negation. This is further supported by the principle of generic convention (wad' naw'i) where the rule is rationally derived. The passage then turns to the qualifier 'non-enumerated' explaining that it means the word does not specify a particular number. The author responds to an objection that this qualifier is redundant because number nouns are already excluded by the qualifier 'comprehensive of that which it is suitable for' (istighraq lima yaslahu lahu). However, the author argues that number nouns actually are suitable for each unit by implicational indication, and thus, under that interpretation, numbers would enter the definition. The discussion clarifies that the suitability intended is like that of a universal to its particulars or a whole to its parts, and by implication, number nouns do indicate individuals. The passage ends mid-argument, likely continuing the response to the objection. Throughout, the Hanafi position is upheld, emphasizing that the generality of the negated indefinite is a reality, not a metaphor, and that the definition of general requires the condition of non-enumeration to exclude definitive numbers.

Key Terms
نکرہ منفیہ (naqira manfiya): An indefinite noun occurring in a context of negation; it indicates generality of negation (i.e., negation of every individual).
وضع نوعی (wad' naw'i): Generic convention: a rule set by the language coiner that applies to a whole category of words, derived rationally.
استغراق لما یصلح لہ (istighraq lima yaslahu lahu): Comprehensiveness of all that the word is suitable to apply to; a defining characteristic of a general term.
کثیر غیر محصور (kathir ghayr mahsur): Non-enumerated many: a multitude that is not specifiable by a fixed number.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in this passage, the generality indicated by the negated indefinite (naqira manfiya) is considered:
A) Majaz (metaphorical) because the indefinite originally means a vague individual.
B) Haqiqah (reality) because it is used in its original meaning and the generality is derived rationally from negation.
C) Only applies when the indefinite is plural.
D) A weak form of generality that can be overridden by context.

Key Takeaway
The negated indefinite is a real general, not metaphorical; and the definition of general requires a non-enumerated many, which excludes number nouns but includes plurals and collectives.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
8 min
Definition of 'Aam and the Status of Jama' Munkar
Debating the definition of 'aam and the status of jam' munkar in Hanafi usul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'aam requiring istighraq, identify why ghayr mahsur is rejected, and distinguish the dispute over whether jam' munkar is 'aam or an intermediate (wasitah).

Core Idea
Jama' munkar is not automatically 'aam; the key condition is istighraq (comprehensive coverage).

This passage addresses a central debate in Hanafi usul: what makes a word truly 'aam? The author argues that the condition of 'ghayr mahsur' (uncounted) is flawed, and the correct condition is istighraq lima yaslihu lahu. He then applies this to jam' munkar (indefinite plural), where there is a difference of opinion: some scholars treat it as always 'aam, while the author and the muhaqqiqun consider it wasitah unless istighraq is present.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target definition
The passage begins by critiquing the definition of 'aam as covering a large uncounted multitude. This sets up the debate.
Focus on the term 'ghayr mahsur'.
2
Analyze the refutation
Two objections are raised: the word 'heavens' (counted) would lose generality, and the number 'thousand' would become general. Both are contradictions to established fiqh.
Notice the use of examples from the Quran and Arabic usage.
3
Understand the correct condition
The author replaces 'ghayr mahsur' with 'istighraq lima yaslihu lahu', explaining that numbers are excluded by inability to apply to individual units by mutabaqah, while plurals like 'al-muslimun' apply by tadmin.
Distinguish between dalaalah bi al-mutabaqah and dalaalah bi al-tadmin.
4
Apply to jam' munkar
The author stipulates istighraq for 'aam; without it, jam' munkar becomes wasitah bayn al-'aam wa al-khass, as per the muhaqqiqun.
Contrast with the view of Fakhr al-Islam.
5
Evaluate the objection
An objection is raised that wasitah status is not exclusive to jam' munkar, but the author's response is that a restricted 'aam remains 'aam in the remaining individuals.
This defense is consistent with the author's explicit statement.

مستغرق الا ان هذا غير مختص به مصنف پر اعتراض ہے کہ قول بالواسطه بين العام والخاص حضرت اسی جمع منکر کے ساتھ مختص نہیں رہتا بلکہ ہر وہ عام جو مقصور علی البعض ہو خواہ اس کا قصر علی البعض بدليل العقل ہو یا اس کے علاوہ اور کسی وجہ سے مثلا استثنا سے تو یہ بھی واسطہ ہو جائیں گے خواہ یہ جمع منکر ہو یا اس جیسی کوئی اور شی کیونکہ مصنف کا قول وان لم يستغرق فجمع منکر ونحوہ اس کو شامل ہے دفسادہ بین کیونکہ یہ عام ہے نہ کہ واسطہ بین العام والخاص اجیب ان فسادہ لیں بہتین کیونکہ مصنف نے خود تصریح کردی ہے کہ وہ عام جس سے بعض افراد مخصوص کئے گئے بدلیل عقل یا بالاستثناء وہ باقی افراد میں حقیقت ہوتا ہے یعنی یہاں وہ اپنے
It is that which comprehensively encompasses a large uncounted [multitude]. It is not said that the meaning of 'uncounted' is that the proponent is making a second meaning. The summary is that by 'uncounted' are meant things that cannot be encompassed under quantification and counting; this is called 'uncounted'. We refute this by saying: if this meaning is intended, then the word 'heavens' would become a definite plural form (al-samawāt) placed for a counted [limited] thing because the heavens are only seven, as explicitly mentioned in the Noble Qur'an. Then in that case the word 'heavens' would not be general, whereas it is, by consensus, general. A second objection is that according to this meaning, the word 'thousand' would be placed for a large uncounted [multitude], because the Arabs were an illiterate nation who did not know calculation and bookkeeping, so such a large number could not be enumerated by them. Then it would become general, whereas it is a number word which is not general but rather a counterpart of the general. It is not said that this condition is redundant, i.e. the condition 'uncounted' is unnecessary and superfluous, because the purpose is only to exclude number, and number is already excluded by the condition 'istighraq limā yaslihu lahu' because a number word like 'one hundred' has the capability to apply to its parts, i.e. it can apply to hundreds, but it does not have the capability to apply to the individual units that fall under that number, because the word 'one hundred' cannot apply to each single unit. Therefore, with the condition 'istighraq limā yaslihu lahu' number is excluded from the general, so there is no need for the condition 'uncounted'. We reply by saying: the intended meaning of capability is the kind of capability that a universal noun has to apply to its particulars, or 'the whole to its parts' like 'kull' applies to its parts; now 'kull' is not predicated on each individual part but rather the totality of parts is called 'kull'. So he considered the signification, i.e. the word signifies those things for which it has capability, whether by conformity or inclusion; thus in the word 'one hundred' there exists the capability of the whole to its parts and signification by inclusion upon the parts. By this consideration, the plural forms become comprehensive; and by this consideration, plural forms like 'al-muslimūn', 'al-rijāl', and singular plurals like 'rahṭ' and 'qawm' are comprehensive of all that they are capable of applying to, because in them too the capability with respect to individuals is the same capability of applying the whole to the parts, and their signification is also inclusion. So in that respect, number intervenes; in that case, the word 'mustaghriq' is nominative and a quality of the word, and 'istighraq' means that it encompasses and includes all the individuals that it has capability for, taking into account external indications. His statement: 'and if not, it is a munkar plural', meaning if that word does not comprehensively encompass all that it is capable of, then it is a munkar plural. 'What is considered in the general...' meaning according to Fakhr al-Islam and some scholars, in the general only this is considered: that it includes a group of named things with consideration of a common attribute, i.e. by that attribute they share equally in the group, whether or not istighraq exists, i.e. whether it comprehensively encompasses all that it is capable of or not; the munkar plural is general in their view, meaning the munkar plural, whether comprehensive or not, is in every case general according to these scholars. And since the author stipulated, i.e. the author stipulated istighraq in the definition of the general, which is also the choice of the verifiers, then in their view the munkar plural becomes an intermediate between the general and the specific, according to those who deny istighraq; but for those who affirm istighraq, it is not an intermediate. On this assumption, when they hold that the munkar plural is general, then the author's statement 'and if not, it is a munkar plural' refers to the munkar plural where there is an indication that it is not contradictory, such as 'I saw men today' or 'in the house are men'; here there is an indication that 'men' does not comprehensively encompass all individual men, because seeing all men in one day is impossible, nor can all men come in the house; therefore at that time it becomes an intermediate between the general and the specific. It will enter, so to exclude it the condition 'uncounted' is necessary. His statement 'comprehensive'... except that this is not exclusive to it: an objection against the author is that the status of intermediate between the general and the specific is not exclusive to the munkar plural; rather, every general that is restricted to some [individuals], whether its restriction is by rational evidence or by something else, for example by exception, then these too would be intermediates, whether it is a munkar plural or something else, because the author's statement 'if not comprehensive, then it is a munkar plural or the like' includes it. Its corruption is apparent because it is general, not an intermediate. The answer: its corruption is due to both, because the author himself explicitly stated that a general which is restricted to some individuals by rational evidence or by exception is still a reality in the remaining individuals, meaning that here it is in its
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is an extended discussion on the definition of 'aam (general) in Hanafi usul, specifically focusing on the condition of istighraq (comprehensive coverage) and the status of jam' munkar (indefinite plural). The author (likely the commentator on al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih) begins by critiquing the condition of 'ghayr mahsur' (uncounted) in defining 'aam. He points out that if 'uncounted' is understood as 'not subject to enumeration', then plural nouns like 'al-samawat' (the heavens) would not be general because the heavens are counted (seven), yet they are universally agreed to be general. Similarly, numerical nouns like 'alf' (thousand) would become general, whereas they are not 'aam. Thus the condition is rejected. The author then explains that the proper condition is 'istighraq lima yaslihu lahu' (comprehensive coverage of all that the word is capable of applying to), which excludes numbers because numbers like 'mi'ah' (hundred) cannot apply to individual units by conformity (mutabaqah) but only by inclusion (tadmin). This distinction is critical: for plural forms like 'al-muslimun', they have the capability to apply to each individual (by tadmin), hence they qualify as 'aam with istighraq. The passage then shifts to a discussion of jam' munkar. According to Fakhr al-Islam and other leading scholars, jam' munkar is considered 'aam regardless of whether it has istighraq or not. However, the author of the matn (Sadr al-Shariah) stipulates istighraq for 'aam, so in his view jam' munkar without istighraq is an intermediate (wasitah) between 'aam and khass. The commentator defends this position by explaining that jam' munkar with a qarinah (indication) limiting it becomes wasitah, as in the examples 'ra'aytu al-yawma rijalan' (I saw men today) where complete coverage is impossible. The final part addresses an objection: the status of wasitah is not exclusive to jam' munkar but applies to any 'aam restricted by a rational evidence or exception. The commentator responds that the author already clarified that such a restricted 'aam remains 'aam in the remaining individuals, so it is not a wasitah but genuine 'aam. Thus, the passage provides a refined definition of 'aam and clarifies the Hanafi position on jam' munkar.

Key Terms
عام ('aam): A word that comprehensively includes all individuals for which it is capable (mustaghriq lima yaslihu lahu), according to the author's preferred definition.
جمع منكر (jam' munkar): An indefinite plural noun, like 'rijal' (men), which does not begin with 'al' nor is it in a genitive construction making it definite.
استغراق (istighraq): Comprehensive coverage of all individuals that the word is capable of applying to, as a condition for being truly 'aam.
غير محصور (ghayr mahsur): Uncounted, not subject to enumeration; a condition that the author rejects in defining 'aam because it leads to contradictions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author (muhaqqiqun), what is a necessary condition for a word to be considered 'aam?
A) It must have the form of a plural.
B) It must be definite (with al- or possessive).
C) It must have istighraq (comprehensive coverage of all its possible referents).
D) It must be uncounted (ghayr mahsur).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'aam requires istighraq; jam' munkar without it is an intermediate between general and specific.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
The Condition of Istighraq in Defining 'Amm
Expanding on the definition of 'amm and the condition of istighraq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'amm, explain the condition of istighraq, and distinguish when an indefinite plural (jami' munkar) is 'amm or wasitah.

Core Idea
For Hanafi verifiers, 'amm must encompass all its potential individuals — without istighraq, it is not truly general.

This lesson unpacks a foundational dispute in Hanafi usul: does the term 'amm (general) require absolute encompassment (istighraq) of all individuals it could cover, or is it enough that it includes a group by a common attribute? The answer determines whether an indefinite plural like 'rijalun' is treated as general or as an intermediate category (wasitah). We will examine both positions and their practical consequences for legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Define 'amm and istighraq
State the lexical and technical meaning of 'amm: a word that indicates all individuals it is qualified to include. Istighraq means encompassing every such individual by indication (dalalah).
The commentary clarifies that external evidence (qara'in) may restrict the actual scope.
2
Contrast the two main positions
Identify the muhaqqiqun (verifiers) who require istighraq as a condition, and Fakhr al-Islam with some mashayikh who only require inclusion of a group by a common attribute.
This is the pivotal ikhtilaf in the passage.
3
Apply to jami' munkar (indefinite plural) with and without istighraq
Test the definitions: e.g., 'ra'aytu rijalan' (I saw men) has a qarinah that it cannot encompass all men, so it lacks istighraq. According to muhaqqiqun, it is wasitah (intermediate), not 'amm.
When a qarinah indicates non-encompassment, jami' munkar becomes wasitah.
4
Examine the objection and response about wasitah
An objector argues that any 'amm restricted by reason or exception should also be wasitah. The response: such a restricted 'amm is still used in its real meaning for the remaining individuals, so it remains 'amm.
The restriction does not change the fundamental nature of the word.
5
Understand the shari'i type distinction
The passage ends by noting that Shari'ah may treat words as different types based on legal effects, e.g., rajul (man) and imra'ah (woman) are separate shari'i types despite being one logical type.
This shows that 'type' in usul is not purely logical but purpose-driven.

Meaning: the word 'mustaghriq' is an adjective of the word, and istighraq means that all individuals for which it is qualified are encompassed and included by indication, taking external evidence into account. His statement 'and if not encompassing, then it is jami' munkar': meaning if the word does not encompass all that it is suitable for, then it is a jami' munkar. The consideration in 'amm... meaning according to Fakhr al-Islam and some mashayikh, only this is considered in 'amm: that it includes a group of namesakes by virtue of a common matter, i.e., it shares equally in that group by virtue of that matter, whether or not istighraq exists. The indefinite plural according to them is 'amm. And the author, since he conditioned istighraq... meaning the author placed the condition of istighraq in the definition of 'amm, as is the choice of the verifiers, so according to them, jami' munkar will be wasitah bayn al-'amm wa al-khass according to those who hold that there is no istighraq; but according to those who hold that there is istighraq, jami' munkar will be 'amm. So on this estimation, when according to them jami' munkar is 'amm, then in the author's statement 'and if not encompassing, then jami' munkar', jami' munkar means that where there is an indication that it is not encompassing, like 'I saw men today' or 'in the house are men' — here there is an indication that 'men' is not encompassing all men, because it is not possible to see all men in one day, nor can all men come into a house. Hence at that time it becomes wasitah bayn al-'amm wa al-khass. If it enters, then for its exclusion the restriction of ghayr mahsur is necessary. His statement: 'mustaghriq' — except that this is not specific to it. An objection against the author: that the ruling of wasitah bayn al-'amm wa al-khass is not specific to jami' munkar, but rather every 'amm that is restricted to some, whether its restriction is by rational evidence or by another cause like exception, these will also be wasitah. Whether it is jami' munkar or any other thing like it, because the author's statement 'and if it does not encompass, then jami' munkar and the like' includes it. Its corruption is apparent because it is 'amm, not wasitah. The answer: its corruption is clear because the author himself has stated that the 'amm from which some individuals are excluded by rational evidence or by exception, it is used in its real meaning in the remaining individuals. Therefore it is 'amm, not wasitah. His statement: 'or by consideration of type, like rajul and faras' — indication... meaning in these two examples, the author indicated that the type in the custom of Shari'ah, i.e., the shari'i type, sometimes is the same as the logical type, like faras, and sometimes is not the logical type, like rajul, because Shari'ah considers rajul and mar'ah as two different types. He looked at the specifics... meaning in the view of Shari'ah, the difference of types is based on the difference of purposes. Since the purposes of the creation of rajul are that he is a son, great and small imamah, and testimony in hadd and qisas — these matters are specific to rajul, and a woman does not have these rulings. Therefore, due to the difference of these specificities, rajul is a separate type and woman is a separate type.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of 'amm (general) from the matn. It begins by explaining that 'amm is a word that indicates all its potential individuals, but external evidence may restrict it. The key point of contention is whether 'amm requires absolute istighraq (encompassment) or not. The author of the matn (al-Quduri? or the text being commented on) requires istighraq, following the muhaqqiqin (verifiers). However, Fakhr al-Islam and other mashayikh define 'amm simply as a word that includes a group of individuals by a common attribute, regardless of istighraq. According to them, even the indefinite plural (jami' munkar) is 'amm whether it is mustaghriq or not. The matn author's view leads to a category of 'wasitah bayn al-'amm wa al-khass' (intermediate between general and specific), which is a jami' munkar that does not have istighraq, like 'ra'aytu rijalan' (I saw men) — since it cannot realistically encompass all men, it is not truly 'amm but a middle ground. An objector argues that this wasitah status is not exclusive to jami' munkar but applies to any 'amm that is restricted by reason or exception. The response is that such a restricted 'amm is still used in its real meaning (haqiqi) for the remaining individuals, so it remains 'amm. The passage then discusses the shari'i type (naw') and how Shari'ah treats rajul (man) and faras (horse) differently: faras is a logical type, but rajul is a shari'i type distinct from imra'ah (woman) because of different rulings and purposes. This shows that Shari'ah creates its own categories based on legal effects.

Key Terms
العام (al-'amm): A word that encompasses all its individuals by virtue of a common attribute; requires istighraq according to the muhaqqiqun.
الاستغراق (al-istighraq): Encompassing all individuals that the word is qualified to include.
جمع منكر (jami' munkar): Indefinite plural (e.g., 'rijalun' without definite article).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the muhaqqiqun (verifiers), what is the status of the indefinite plural 'rijal' in 'ra'aytu rijalan' (I saw men)?
A) It is 'amm (general)
B) It is wasitah bayn al-'amm wa al-khass (intermediate)
C) It is khass (specific)
D) It is mujmal (ambiguous)

Key Takeaway
The definition of 'amm in Hanafi usul requires istighraq; without it, an indefinite plural becomes wasitah bayn al-'amm wa al-khass.
